### Notebook 08 — Rollout DiD

**Grade: adequate** · 1 critical · 4 major · 9 findings total

| Diagnostics | Business | Narration | Validation | Watertight |
|:--:|:--:|:--:|:--:|:--:|
| 3 | 3 | 4 | 2 | 2 |

Notebook 08 has excellent bones — an honest parallel-trends exposition, a clean 7-step structure, and a genuinely correct, advanced staggered-adoption/TWFE-bias demo (verified: true weighted post-treatment effect is exactly EUR 400, naive TWFE EUR 177) — but its central validate step fails and mislabels the failure. The Bayesian DiD posterior (EUR 327, 90% [302, 353]) excludes both the simulated truth (EUR 400) and the hand-computed 2x2 (EUR 390), yet the figure is titled "Posterior recovers truth" and the markdown calls the discrepancy "mildly shrink". Root cause is CausalPy 0.8.1's fixed LinearRegression priors (beta ~ Normal(0, 50), sigma ~ HalfNormal(1)) applied to EUR-1000-scale data, compounded by unaddressed r-hat > 1.01 / ESS < 100 warnings in the committed output. Every euro number downstream (net EUR 207/store/week, annual EUR 5.39M with an interval excluding the truth-implied EUR 7.28M, break-even EUR 302) inherits the corrupted posterior. Secondary gaps: the event study is descriptive (no CIs) despite claiming a "formal" check; there is no calibration or multi-seed recovery; the pilot-store ATT is silently extrapolated to 500 stores; and the "CausalPy requires the 2x2 form" claim is not actually true of the 0.8.1 API. All fixable — demeaning/rescaling the outcome before the fit (or passing data-scaled priors) would recenter the posterior on ~390-400 and likely resolve the convergence warnings at the same time.

**Strengths.**
- The staggered-adoption/TWFE trap demo (cell 13) is statistically correct and rare at MBA level: two cohorts (weeks 8/16), growing dynamic effects, a genuine unit+time fixed-effects regression via lstsq; the true weighted average post-treatment effect works out to exactly EUR 400 while naive TWFE gives EUR 177 — a textbook-clean, live demonstration of forbidden comparisons / negative weighting (Goodman-Bacon), with the correct remedies (Callaway-Sant'Anna, Sun-Abraham) named. Delivers exactly what the README promises.
- The parallel-trends narration is honest and precise: explicitly flagged as untestable post-launch, pre-trends framed as evidence rather than proof, a good 'how to read the event study' cell, and a correct escape route ('a diverging pre-trend kills the design — use synthetic control, nb 07'). The two-differences worked formula and the beta_3 regression form are both stated correctly.
- The decide-in-euros step is internally consistent and traceable: net = impact - 120 (327 - 120 = 207 OK), annual = net x 500 x 52 (EUR 5.39M OK), P(pays) = 1.00 OK, break-even = 5th percentile = EUR 302 OK — every printed number matches the draws, and the posterior-to-decision pipeline is a clean single chain of transformations an MBA audience can follow.

<details><summary>Axis notes</summary>

- **Diagnostics (3/5).** Event study, placebo-in-time, 2x2 cross-check, and an excellent TWFE demo are present, but the event study carries no uncertainty (no lead/lag CIs, no noise benchmark for the EUR 22 pre-gap), the placebo is a single split with no scale, there is no posterior predictive check, and committed r-hat/ESS warnings go unaddressed.
- **Business (3/5).** Net/annual/P(pays)/break-even are all computed correctly from the draws, but it is one cost point (EUR 120) with no cost/adoption sweep, no break-even table, no VOI, and the pilot-store ATT is extrapolated to 500 stores without an external-validity note.
- **Narration (4/5).** Best axis: two-differences math, honest parallel-trends framing, event-study reading guide, Card-Krueger anchor, forbidden-comparisons explanation, and accurate cross-link to nb 07; docked for never naming the ATT estimand, never defining 'credible interval', and misattributing the 2x2 collapse to an API constraint.
- **Validation (2/5).** The recover-the-truth step demonstrably does NOT recover the truth (90% interval [302, 353] excludes EUR 400) yet is labeled as success; no calibration, no coverage check, no multi-seed recovery — a 20-seed loop of the cheap 2x2 would have exposed the failure.
- **Watertight (2/5).** The DiD math, placebo, and TWFE demo are correct, but the headline posterior is biased by mis-scaled default priors, its interval excludes the truth while the notebook claims recovery, r-hat/ESS warnings are unaddressed, and 'we check this formally' overstates a descriptive plot — each a hole a sharp audience member would find.

</details>

- **🔴 CRITICAL · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cells 7-9 and the cell-9 figure (PDF page 3); src/cmp/estimators.py:369-376; .venv-legacy/.../causalpy/pymc_models.py:503-510; src/cmp/dgp.py:320-321`
  - **Issue.** The validate step fails to recover the known truth and the notebook mislabels the failure as success. The Bayesian DiD posterior mean is EUR 327 with 90% interval [302, 353], which excludes both the simulated truth (EUR 400) and the hand-computed 2x2 on the same data (EUR 390, within 1 sampling-sd of truth). Yet the histogram is titled 'Posterior recovers truth; placebo ~ 0' — with the truth line visibly outside the posterior mass on PDF page 3 — and the cell-8 markdown calls it 'the priors mildly shrink it'. A 16% bias whose interval excludes the truth is not mild shrinkage; it is a prior-misspecification artifact: CausalPy 0.8.1's LinearRegression uses fixed priors beta ~ Normal(0, 50) and sigma ~ HalfNormal(1) (no priors_from_data override), catastrophically mis-scaled for revenue ~ EUR 1000-1400 and an effect of EUR 400. The dgp docstring (dgp.py:320-321) shows the authors knew default priors were scale-sensitive but the problem was not resolved.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 7 output: 'DiD effect EUR 327/store/week (true EUR 400) . 90% CI [EUR 302, EUR 353]'. Cell 9 output: 'Bayesian EUR 327 . 2x2 EUR 390 . true EUR 400'. Figure title in cell 9 code: 'Posterior recovers truth; placebo ~ 0'. CausalPy source confirms static default_priors = beta Normal(0,50), y_hat sigma HalfNormal(1) (pymc_models.py:503-510), and PyMCModel.fit merges priors_from_data which LinearRegression does not implement (pymc_models.py:261).
  - **Why it matters.** This is the notebook's own step-5 contract ('recover-the-truth') failing on the committed run, on the most visually inspectable figure in the deck. Any student who reads the histogram sees the dashed 'true EUR 400' line outside the blue mass under a title claiming recovery — exactly the 'broken headline claim' that embarrasses in a lecture. It also corrupts everything downstream (see the euro-decision finding).
  - **Fix.** Demean or standardize revenue before the CausalPy fit and back-transform the interaction draws (or pass data-scaled priors via the model's priors argument). The posterior will then center at ~390-400 and the title becomes true. Alternatively, keep the miss and turn it into an explicit teaching moment about default-prior scale sensitivity — but then retitle the figure, rewrite 'mildly shrink', and base the euro decision on a corrected fit.
  - **Verifier.** Every factual citation was verified against the artifacts. (1) Notebook 08 cell 7 committed output reads 'DiD effect €327/store/week (true €400) · 90% CI [€302, €353]'; cell 9 prints 'Bayesian €327 · 2x2 €390 · true €400 · placebo €17'; the cell-9 figure title is 'Posterior recovers truth; placebo ≈ 0'; cell-8 markdown says 'the priors mildly shrink it'. The PDF page-3 figure visibly shows both the true-€400 dashed line and the 2×2 €390 dotted line outside the entire posterior histogram mass, directly under the 'recovers truth' title. (2) The installed CausalPy is 0.8.1; LinearRegression (pymc_models.py class at line 466) has static default_priors beta ~ Normal(0,50) and y_hat sigma ~ HalfNo…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 7 committed stderr outputs; src/cmp/estimators.py:349-357 (_sk, FAST_CP)`
  - **Issue.** The committed run emits 'The rhat statistic is larger than 1.01 for some parameters. This indicates problems during sampling' and 'The effective sample size per chain is smaller than 100', plus the 2-chains recommendation warning — and the notebook never mentions or addresses them, then quotes a 90% interval, P(app pays)=1.00, and a break-even from those same 400 total draws.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 7 stderr (committed): 'Sampling 2 chains for 200 tune and 200 draw iterations (400 + 400 draws total)...', 'The rhat statistic is larger than 1.01...', 'The effective sample size per chain is smaller than 100...'. FAST_CP = {draws: 200, tune: 200, chains: 2} in estimators.py:356.
  - **Why it matters.** The ground rules for this cookbook treat visible-but-unaddressed convergence warnings as top-priority statistical defects. Quoting interval endpoints to the euro from a run the sampler itself flags as unreliable undermines the 'watertight' bar; a technically literate audience member reading the notebook will see the red stderr blocks directly above the headline number.
  - **Fix.** Note that the poor geometry is driven by the mis-scaled sigma ~ HalfNormal(1) prior fighting EUR-150-scale residuals — fixing the prior/scaling (critical finding) will likely clear r-hat. Then either commit a FULL-mode run (1000/1000, 4 chains) for the lecture PDF or add one sentence acknowledging FAST-mode sampling and showing az.summary r-hat/ESS to prove the final run converged.
  - **Verifier.** Every factual citation verifies against the artifacts. (1) The committed Cell 7 stderr of notebooks/08_rollout_did.ipynb contains verbatim the three warnings quoted: 2 chains / 400+400 draws, "rhat statistic is larger than 1.01 ... indicates problems during sampling", and "effective sample size per chain is smaller than 100", plus the 4-chains recommendation (extract lines 142-177). (2) src/cmp/estimators.py:356 is exactly FAST_CP = {"draws": 200, "tune": 200, "chains": 2}, passed via _sk (lines 349-353) into did() (line 375). (3) A full-text search of the notebook finds zero acknowledgment of the warnings, FAST-mode sampling limits, r-hat, ESS, or az.summary; the Step 7 caveats cell covers …

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 0 ('We check this formally with an event study'), cells 4-5 and the cell-5 printout`
  - **Issue.** The event study is purely descriptive but is sold as a formal check. It plots raw weekly treated-minus-control gaps re-centered on the pre-period mean, with no confidence bands on the pre-launch leads, no joint test, and no noise benchmark. The printed line 'Pre-launch gaps average EUR 22 (~0 supports parallel trends)' is doubly muddy: (a) the centered gaps average exactly zero by construction, so what is printed is the mean ABSOLUTE deviation, and (b) EUR 22 is given no reference scale — under pure noise (sigma=80, 20 stores/group) the expected mean absolute gap is ~EUR 20, so EUR 22 is exactly what noise predicts, but the notebook never establishes this.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 5 code: gap_centered = gap - gap[pre].mean(); pre_gap = gap_centered[pre].abs().mean(); print('Pre-launch gaps average EUR 22 (~0 supports parallel trends)'). No interval or test appears anywhere in the cell; the right panel of the PDF page-2 figure shows bare dots.
  - **Why it matters.** Parallel trends is, as the notebook itself says, 'the whole ballgame'. A sharp audience member will ask 'is EUR 22 small? compared to what?' and the notebook has no answer. The README advertises an 'event-study pre-trends check'; the standard version of that check is lead/lag coefficients with intervals, and its absence is the signature-diagnostic gap for this method.
  - **Fix.** Fit the standard event-study regression (revenue on unit FE, week FE, and treated x week-relative-to-launch dummies, omitting week -1) and plot lead/lag coefficients with 90% intervals — cheap OLS, no sampling needed. State the noise benchmark for the pre-gap (expected ~EUR 20 under H0 here). Change 'we check this formally' to match whatever is actually shown.
  - **Verifier.** Factual accuracy: verified against the notebook extract and PDF. Cell 0 says "We check this formally with an event study" (lines 30-31); cell 5 contains exactly the quoted code (gap_centered = gap - pre-mean; pre_gap = gap_centered[pre].abs().mean(); print '...average €22 (≈0 supports parallel trends)') with no interval, joint test, or benchmark anywhere in the notebook; the PDF page-2 right panel shows bare dots with no confidence bands; README lines 57-58 advertise 'event-study pre-trends' for nb 08. Statistical soundness: (a) the centered pre-period gaps average exactly zero by construction, so the printed €22 is indeed the mean ABSOLUTE deviation mislabeled as an average; (b) the noise b…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cells 8-9 (step 5) vs the 7-step contract and the four-axes bar`
  - **Issue.** No calibration or multi-seed recovery anywhere: step 5 consists of one same-seed 2x2 cross-check and one placebo split (week 6). There is no coverage check (does the 90% interval contain the truth across seeds?), no seed-stability loop, and no second placebo date.
  - **Evidence.** Cells 8-9 contain only did_2x2, one placebo, and the histogram; grep of the extract shows no seed loop, no coverage computation, and no reliability/calibration call from cmp.metrics.
  - **Why it matters.** The repo's own contract for step 5 is 'recover-the-truth + calibration'. In this notebook the omission is not academic: a 20-seed loop of the cheap 2x2 (pure groupby arithmetic, no MCMC) plus the Bayesian fit on a handful of seeds would have immediately revealed that the Bayesian interval essentially never covers EUR 400 — i.e., it would have caught the critical finding before it reached the PDF.
  - **Fix.** Add a multi-seed cell: for seed in range(20): simulate, compute the 2x2, record it; report mean/sd and the fraction of runs where the (corrected) Bayesian 90% interval covers EUR 400. Add a second placebo split (e.g., week 4 and week 8) to show robustness rather than a single point.
  - **Verifier.** Factually accurate: step 5 (cells 8-9 of 08_rollout_did) contains only the same-seed 2x2 cross-check, a single week-6 placebo split, and a histogram. The only DGP seed is SEED=23 (cell 1); there is no seed loop, no coverage computation, and no call to cmp.metrics.interval_coverage or reliability_curve, both of which exist in src/cmp/metrics.py (lines 23 and 94) and are used by other notebooks (01, 03 contain coverage/seed work), confirming the repo bar is applied elsewhere but not here. Statistically sound: the criticism identifies a real, consequential gap — the committed output in cell 7 shows the Bayesian 90% CI [EUR 302, EUR 353] EXCLUDES the true EUR 400 (point estimate EUR 327 vs 2x2's…

- **🟠 MAJOR · business** — `Cell 4 (identification), cells 10-12 (decide + caveats)`
  - **Issue.** The estimand is never stated as a causal quantity — cell 4 defines DiD only mechanically as 'beta_3 in the regression' — and the decide step then silently extrapolates the 20-pilot-store ATT to all 500 chain stores (N_STORES=500) with no effect-homogeneity or external-validity caveat; cell 12's caveats cover parallel trends, spillover, and staggered adoption but not this. The decision itself is also thin against the bar: a single APP_COST=120 point, one scalar break-even, no cost/adoption sweep, no treat-all-vs-pilot-further policy table, no value-of-information.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 4: 'DiD estimates beta_3 in Y = ...' with no ATT statement. Cell 11: N_STORES, WEEKS, APP_COST = 500, 52, 120.0; annual = net * N_STORES * WEEKS. Cell 12 caveats list: parallel trends, spillover, staggered adoption only.
  - **Why it matters.** Under parallel trends, DiD identifies the average treatment effect on the TREATED stores in the post period — the pilot stores, which may be systematically different from the other 480 (chains rarely pick pilot sites at random). Multiplying the pilot ATT by 500 stores is precisely the leap an MBA audience should be taught to interrogate, and the projected EUR 5.4M/year headline hides it. The euro step is also the notebook's weakest of the seven relative to the 'sweeps / break-even tables / policy comparison' bar met elsewhere in the repo.
  - **Fix.** In step 3, state: 'under parallel trends, beta_3 identifies the ATT — the effect on the pilot stores'. In step 6, add one caveat sentence on extrapolating to 480 non-pilot stores, plus a small sweep: P(app pays) and annual value as a function of APP_COST (e.g., 80-300) and of a partial-transfer discount on the effect (e.g., 100%/75%/50% of pilot ATT) — a 3x5 table would do.

- **🟡 MINOR · consistency** — `Cell 6 markdown ('expects the classic 2x2 form — exactly one before and one after observation per unit'); README.md:222-223; .venv-legacy/.../causalpy/experiments/diff_in_diff.py:266-282`
  - **Issue.** The claim that CausalPy 0.8.1's DifferenceInDifferences requires the 2x2 collapsed form is factually wrong about the API. input_validation() only requires post_treatment in the formula and data plus a 'unit' column; algorithm() explicitly handles 'multiple units per time point' and multiple periods, and the interaction coefficient would be estimated on the full 40x24 panel just as well. The README repeats the same claim ('DiD needs ... the 2x2 (pre/post-collapsed) data form').
  - **Evidence.** diff_in_diff.py:266-282 validates only post_treatment (formula + column) and the unit column; the x_pred_* blocks at lines 172-227 group by the time variable and take .first(), i.e., are panel-aware. Nothing asserts two time points.
  - **Why it matters.** A causalpy-literate attendee (or anyone who reads the library docs) can falsify the claim in one minute. More importantly, it wastes the real teaching point: collapsing to pre/post means is the Bertrand-Duflo-Mullainathan (2004) fix for serially correlated errors in DiD — the statistically principled reason to do exactly what the notebook does.
  - **Fix.** Rewrite the cell-6 sentence: 'We collapse each store to one pre-mean and one post-mean — the classic fix for serially correlated weekly errors (Bertrand, Duflo & Mullainathan 2004) — which also gives CausalPy the clean 2x2 it plots best.' Fix the README line to match (a modeling choice, not an API requirement).

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cells 7 and 11 ('90% CI', 'Break-even: the app stays profitable up to a running cost of ~EUR 302/store/week')`
  - **Issue.** Interval language is loose for a Bayesian cookbook: '90% CI' is never defined as a posterior credible interval (first use, cell 7), and the break-even statement drops its probability qualifier — EUR 302 is the cost at which P(effect > cost) = 0.95, i.e., 'profitable with 95% posterior probability', not 'stays profitable up to'.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 7 print: '90% CI [EUR 302, EUR 353]' with no definition anywhere in the notebook; cell 11 print: 'the app stays profitable up to a running cost of ~EUR 302/store/week (5th pct of the effect)'.
  - **Why it matters.** The repo's bar is that posterior/interval language be used correctly and terms be defined on first use; conflating credible and confidence intervals in front of an audience that has just been taught frequentist DiD invites exactly the confusion the cookbook exists to prevent, and the unqualified break-even overstates certainty.
  - **Fix.** First use: '90% credible interval — 90% posterior probability the true effect lies inside'. Break-even line: 'with 95% posterior probability the app remains profitable up to a running cost of ~EUR 302/store/week'.

- **🟡 MINOR · pedagogical** — `Cells 8-9 (placebo test)`
  - **Issue.** The placebo is computed with the hand groupby-2x2 arithmetic, not the Bayesian pipeline used for the headline estimate, and its value (EUR 17) is reported with no uncertainty scale — the sampling sd of this placebo DiD is ~EUR 15 here, so EUR 17 is ~1 sd, but the reader has no way to know that 'EUR 17' counts as '~ 0'.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 9: placebo = (mp[1,1]-mp[1,0]) - (mp[0,1]-mp[0,0]) — pure pandas, no est.did call; print: 'placebo (no real effect) EUR 17' with no interval.
  - **Why it matters.** A falsification test should exercise the estimator you actually use (it would also have surfaced the prior-shrinkage problem on a null effect), and '~ 0' claims need a noise yardstick or they read as assertion rather than evidence.
  - **Fix.** Either run est.did on the fake-split pre-period and show its posterior straddling zero, or add one sentence: 'sampling noise for this placebo is ~EUR 15, so EUR 17 is indistinguishable from zero'.

- **⚪ POLISH · pedagogical** — `Cell 13 and PDF page 4 (bar chart); cell 13 code line 'import numpy as _np'`

### Notebook 09 — Threshold perk RDD

**Grade: adequate** · 1 critical · 6 major · 11 findings total

| Diagnostics | Business | Narration | Validation | Watertight |

The core of notebook 09 is statistically sound: the sharp-RD estimand is stated in correct limit notation, locality (LATE at the cutoff) is honestly maintained all the way into the euro step, CausalPy's discontinuity_at_threshold is verified to be the posterior of the mean jump (mu-based, causalpy/experiments/regression_discontinuity.py:230-233), the DGP plants an exact +14pp jump on a linear trend, and the headline +11.3% (90% CI +2.8% to +19.2%) covers the truth with an OLS cross-check agreeing exactly; all printed euro numbers reconcile. But the robustness battery — the notebook's advertised signature — does not meet the watertight bar: the EUR130 placebo cutoff comes out at +5.8% (half the real effect, clearly visible as a tall grey bar in the lecture figure) yet is labeled "(approx 0)" and narrated as "this one doesn't [find a discontinuity at EUR130]"; no placebo or bandwidth estimate carries a standard error, so "approx 0" and "bandwidth-stable" (the curve actually spans +7.2% to +14.9%) are unlicensed claims; two placebo windows straddle the real cutoff; and the "McCrary density test" is a two-bin count ratio with no inference. The committed headline fit shows unaddressed r-hat>1.01 and ESS<100/chain warnings (FAST: 2 chains x 200 draws) beneath the decision quantities P(pays)=0.78 and the EUR6 break-even. The business step promises a perk-cost sweep and an answer to "where to set the threshold?" and delivers neither, and validation is single-seed with no calibration despite the README step-5 promise. Fixes are cheap (SEs or permutation nulls on placebos, FULL-run committed outputs, a 50-seed OLS recovery loop, a cost-sweep curve) and would lift this to lecture grade.

**Strengths.**
- Estimand precision and locality discipline: the sharp-RD estimand is given in limit notation (cell 4), and the LATE-at-the-cutoff scope is maintained consistently from the intro through the euro step ('at the current EUR100 margin — that's what RD identifies', cell 12) to the caveats (don't extrapolate to EUR500 whales) — rare and exactly right.
- Truth recovery is genuine and cross-checked: true +14pp lies inside the 90% interval [+2.8%, +19.2%]; an independent frequentist OLS refit at the same bandwidth reproduces the Bayesian mean (+11.3% both, verified by recomputation); the DGP is deliberately linear so local-linear RD is unbiased, documented at src/cmp/dgp.py:358; sampler and DGP are both seeded (random_seed=1 in _sk, SEED=29), so the notebook is reproducible.
- Lecture-ready pedagogy: the EUR99.50-vs-EUR100.50 comparison is a memorable MBA-grade explanation of the design; the three-panel robustness figure (binned scatter + bandwidth curve + placebo bars) is compact and legible in the PDF, with a dedicated 'how to read' cell; the posterior of the mean jump (not the posterior predictive) is correctly used for the effect distribution and P(perk pays).

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:213-226 (mccrary_density) as used in Cell 6; README.md:58 and :94`
  - **Issue.** What the notebook and README call "the McCrary density test" is a two-bin count comparison (counts within one bandwidth below vs above the cutoff, log((d_above+1)/(d_below+1))) with an arbitrary |log-ratio|<0.3 pass threshold and no standard error or test statistic — not McCrary's local-linear density discontinuity test.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:223-225: d_below = len(below)/bandwidth; log_ratio = log((d_above+1)/(d_below+1)); notebook threshold abs(log_ratio) < 0.3 hard-coded in cell 6; docstring itself says 'Simple McCrary-style' while the notebook headline says 'McCrary density test'.
  - **Why it matters.** A density that slopes smoothly through the cutoff (typical in real CRM spend data) would flag 'manipulation' under this bin comparison even with zero sorting, and conversely the 0.3 threshold has no inferential basis; on real data — which the intro box explicitly invites — this check can both false-alarm and miss. An econometrically literate audience member will recognize it is not the named test. It happens to be harmless here only because spend is simulated symmetric around the cutoff.
  - **Fix.** Either implement a minimal honest version (local-linear density on each side with an SE for the log difference, or an exact binomial test on counts in a symmetric window, which is the Cattaneo-style approximation) or relabel it everywhere — notebook cell 5/6 and README — as a 'simplified McCrary-style density check' with one sentence on what the real test does.
  - **Verifier.** Facts verified: estimators.py:213-226 implements exactly the described two-bin count comparison (counts in one bandwidth each side / bandwidth, log((d_above+1)/(d_below+1))), with no SE or test statistic, and the docstring itself says "Simple McCrary-style". Notebook 09 cells 3/4/5 call it "the McCrary density test" unqualified, cell 6 hard-codes the arbitrary abs(log_ratio)<0.3 pass rule, and README.md:58,94 list "McCrary density" unqualified. Statistics verified: McCrary (2008) is a local-linear density-discontinuity test with a standard error and Wald statistic; the two-bin version is a local-constant approximation whose log-ratio ≈ f'(c)·h/f(c) under a smoothly sloping density — e.g. an …

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 10, line placebos = {c: rd_jump(df, c, 25) for c in [70, 80, 120, 130]}`
  - **Issue.** The placebo windows for EUR80 ([55,105]) and EUR120 ([95,145]) straddle the true EUR100 cutoff, so the local-linear fits on one side of each placebo are forced through the real +14pp discontinuity, contaminating those placebo estimates.
  - **Evidence.** Recomputed: window for c=80 is [55,105] and for c=120 is [95,145], both containing 100; rd_jump treats spend>=c as 'treated' so the segment between 100 and c mixes true-Gold customers into the placebo control side.
  - **Why it matters.** Standard RD practice (Imbens & Lemieux 2008) runs placebo cutoffs at points like the medians of each side using only same-side data precisely to avoid this: a placebo test whose window contains the real jump is neither a clean null nor a clean power check. Here contamination happens to be mild (only ~5 of 25 euros of window cross the cutoff; recomputed estimates -1.2% and +0.9%), but the design flaw is exactly the kind a sharp student will spot, and on real data with a bigger jump it would produce spurious placebo 'effects'.
  - **Fix.** Restrict each placebo regression to observations on one side of the true cutoff (e.g. cutoffs at the below-median ~EUR70 and above-median ~EUR130 with windows capped at 100), or shrink the bandwidth for the near cutoffs; state the one-sided rule in the markdown.
  - **Verifier.** Both prongs hold.

(a) Factually accurate. Cell 10 of the notebook (nb_text extract lines 195-205) defines rd_jump to subset |spend - c| <= bw and code tr = (spend >= c), then runs `placebos = {c: rd_jump(df, c, 25) for c in [70, 80, 120, 130]}` on the FULL df with no one-sided restriction anywhere. So the c=80 window is [55,105] and the c=120 window is [95,145]; both contain the true cutoff at 100 (CUTOFF=100, TRUE=0.14, Cell 1). I recomputed from the seeded DGP (src/cmp/dgp.py::rdd_perk, n=2500, seed=29, exactly as Cell 3): placebo(80) = -1.23% and placebo(120) = +0.89%, matching the reviewer's "-1.2% and +0.9%" to rounding; the notebook's own printed values for 70 (+0.6%) and 130 (+5.8%) …
  - **Corrected statement.** The finding stands as written, with one small precision fix: for the €80 placebo the true-Gold observations (spend in [100,105]) fall on the placebo *treated* side (spend >= 80), not the control side; only for the €120 placebo do true-Gold customers (spend in [100,120)) contaminate the placebo control side. In both cases one local-linear segment straddles the real +14pp jump at €100, which is the substantive flaw.

  - **Evidence.** Cell 8 print: '90% CI [+2.8%, +19.2%]'; NUTS output shows 'y_hat_sigma' Gaussian noise parameter on a 0/1 outcome.
  - **Why it matters.** (a) In a course that elsewhere trades on the credible-vs-confidence distinction, 'CI' invites the classic misinterpretation question. (b) LPM is standard and fine for RD jumps, but a Normal likelihood on 0/1 data with a single sigma is misspecified (Bernoulli variance p(1-p)); one sentence prevents a sharp student from thinking it went unnoticed.
  - **Fix.** Say '90% credible interval' on first use, and add one line in Step 4: 'we model retention with a local linear probability model — standard for RD jumps; a logit link changes nothing material here because the fit is local'.

Also, the fuzzy-RD caveat ('jump in Y / jump in P(treated)') is the natural cross-link to notebook 11's IV/Wald logic, but no cross-reference is made (the four-axes bar asks for cross-links).
  - **Evidence.** Cell 0: 'It estimates a LATE at the cutoff'; Cell 14 fuzzy-RD bullet has no link to 11_endogenous_exposure_iv.
  - **Why it matters.** A methods-trained attendee may quibble with 'LATE' for a sharp design; one footnote and one cross-link ('this ratio is exactly the Wald/IV estimator of notebook 11, with the threshold as instrument') pre-empts it and knits the cookbook together.
  - **Fix.** Add a footnote: 'local here means local-to-the-cutoff (an ATE at EUR100), not the complier-LATE of fuzzy RD — see the fuzzy caveat and notebook 11 for that connection.'

### Notebook 10 — Redesign ITS

**Grade: adequate** · 2 critical · 3 major · 9 findings total

| Diagnostics | Business | Narration | Validation | Watertight |
|:--:|:--:|:--:|:--:|:--:|
| 2 | 2 | 4 | 2 | 2 |

The estimation spine of notebook 10 is sound: the CausalPy ITS recovers the known +3pp step exactly (+3.0%, 90% interval [+2.8%, +3.3%]), the placebo-in-time refit lands at ~0, the identification section is honestly humble (ITS framed as the weakest quasi-experiment, hinging on no-concurrent-shock), and the conceptual narration is lecture-ready. But two issues would embarrass in the lecture: (1) the "residual autocorrelation" diagnostic — one of the two signature diagnostics the README advertises for this notebook — is computed on rolling-7-day-mean deviations of the raw series rather than on model residuals, so the printed lag-1 ACF of 0.42 is just the weekly sin7 seasonality the model already fits (true model-formula residuals are white: max |ACF| 0.11 vs the 0.183 significance band), and the narration then misreads it as evidence the model's intervals may be understated; (2) the entire cell-9 validation figure (cumulative impact / placebo bars / ACF) is missing from the committed notebook and the PDF, yet the "How to read this" cell narrates its three panels in detail. Beyond that, committed outputs carry unaddressed "rhat larger than 1.01 ... problems during sampling" and low-ESS warnings from FAST mode, the euro step is thin (one scenario, no cost, no break-even, a 60-day estimate projected to 365 days with no persistence discounting, P printed as 1.00), and validation is single-seed/single-placebo with no calibration — short of the repo's own four-axes bar.

**Strengths.**
- Clean, honest truth recovery: the ITS estimate +3.0pp with 90% interval [+2.8, +3.3] brackets the known +3pp step, and the placebo-in-time refit on the truncated pre-period (fake day 80) returns +0.0 — the recover-the-truth contract genuinely works end to end (cells 6, 8, 9).
- Flagship-grade conceptual narration: 'the past is the control group', level/trend/seasonality and Fourier terms defined on first use, the forecast band that honestly widens with horizon, ITS explicitly ranked as the weakest quasi-experiment, accurate cross-links to DiD (nb 08) and synthetic control (nb 07), and a concrete 'on real data' paragraph — an MBA audience would follow the idea immediately.
- Careful engineering under the hood: the DGP (dgp.py:368-388) deliberately uses numeric sin7/cos7 Fourier seasonality with a documented rationale (categorical seasonality breaks patsy's pre/post split in CausalPy 0.8.x), and the identification step is kept cleanly separate from estimation with the no-concurrent-shock assumption stated up front rather than buried in caveats.

<details><summary>Axis notes</summary>

- **Diagnostics (2/5).** Signature diagnostics nominally present (placebo-in-time, cumulative impact, ACF) but the ACF is computed on the wrong residuals and misread, the figure showing them is missing from the committed artifact, and the visible CausalPy fit diagnostic (Bayesian R2 0.74) is never discussed.
- **Business (2/5).** Single scenario (8000 visitors x 40 euro x 365 days), no redesign/rollback cost, no break-even table, no persistence/horizon sweep, no VOI; the decision is a trivial KEEP at P=1.00 — far below the repo's own sweeps-and-break-even bar.
- **Narration (4/5).** 'The past is the control group', level/trend/seasonality and Fourier terms defined on first use, honest weakest-quasi-experiment framing, accurate cross-links to nb 07/08, and a concrete on-real-data paragraph — strong, only marred by pp-vs-% looseness.
- **Validation (2/5).** Truth recovery and one placebo work, but it is single-seed, single-placebo-date, no interval-coverage/calibration, no sensitivity range for a concurrent shock, and the autocorrelation honesty check is invalid — README promises multi-seed stability and ranges.
- **Watertight (2/5).** Headline numbers match outputs and the truth is recovered, but a signature diagnostic is computed on the wrong quantity and misinterpreted, and committed outputs show unaddressed 'problems during sampling' r-hat/ESS warnings directly above the headline estimate.

- **🔴 CRITICAL · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 9 (resid = ... rolling(7, center=True).mean() line) and Cell 10 markdown; README.md:59-60`
  - **Issue.** The 'residual autocorrelation' diagnostic is not computed on model residuals. It subtracts a centered 7-day rolling mean from the raw conversion series, which removes trend/level but leaves the weekly sin7 cycle fully intact — the same cycle the ITS model explicitly fits via sin7/cos7. The printed lag-1 ACF of 0.42 is therefore seasonality the model already accounts for, not unmodeled autocorrelation, and cell 10 then misreads it: 'A little autocorrelation remains here ... the naive intervals would be too narrow.'
  - **Evidence.** Committed output cell 9: 'lag-1 residual ACF 0.42'. Reproduction: notebook-style rolling-mean residual ACF(1)=0.42 (exact match), lag-3=-0.68, lag-7=+0.61; model-formula residual ACF max |0.11| < 0.183 band. dgp.py:373-378 shows iid Normal(0, 0.006) noise plus 0.01*sin(2pi t/7) seasonality.
  - **Why it matters.** This is a diagnostic run but misread — top-priority per the review bar. Reproducing the DGP (seed 31): the notebook's rolling-mean 'residuals' have ACF lags 1..7 = [0.42, -0.22, -0.68, -0.59, -0.14, 0.42, 0.61] — a textbook period-7 sinusoid signature (cos(2pi/7)=0.62), while residuals from the actual model formula (OLS of conversion ~ 1 + t + sin7 + cos7 on the pre-period) are white: [0.09, -0.08, -0.07, -0.06, -0.02, 0.11, -0.02], all inside the +/-2/sqrt(120)=0.183 band. The DGP noise is iid (dgp.py:378), so the model's intervals are fine — the notebook manufactures doubt about its own uncertainty with an invalid check, and a sharp audience member who notices the fitted sin7/cos7 terms will dismantle it. The README's claim of 'residual autocorrelation (10)' diagnostics is not actually delivered.
  - **Fix.** Compute residuals from the fitted pre-period model (observed minus posterior-mean prediction on the pre-period, available from the CausalPy result) and plot that ACF — it will be white, which is the correct honest conclusion. Rewrite cell 10 accordingly. If you want to demonstrate the autocorrelation failure mode, add an AR(1)-noise variant of the DGP and show the check catching it.
  - **Verifier.** I attempted to refute the finding and failed on every point; it is factually accurate and statistically sound.

### Notebook 11 — Endogenous exposure IV

**Grade: adequate** · 1 critical · 5 major · 9 findings total

| Diagnostics | Business | Narration | Validation | Watertight |
|:--:|:--:|:--:|:--:|:--:|
| 4 | 3 | 4 | 2 | 2 |

The conceptual and frequentist core is excellent: the OLS / reduced-form / first-stage / Wald decomposition is exactly right and beautifully taught, the first-stage F (156) is correctly computed and interpreted, the exclusion-restriction delta-sweep uses correct algebra ((reduced - delta)/first; break at delta of about 1.4 matches the output), and identification (relevance/exclusion/exogeneity plus Imbens-Angrist monotonicity and LATE scope) is stated with honest testability labels. But the Bayesian headline is not watertight as committed: the '90% CI [15.5, 17.8]' excludes the known truth (15) while the validate step claims recovery. Replicating the exact DGP in numpy shows the honest just-identified IV sampling SD is 2.3 euros (robust 90% CI approx [12.7, 20.3]; 200-seed Wald SD 2.34), so the committed interval is roughly 3.3x overconfident. Root causes are verifiable in source: CausalPy's undisclosed default prior N(2SLS estimate, sd=1) on the exposure coefficient, plus a bivariate-Gaussian likelihood applied to a binary 0/1 exposure (causalpy 0.8.1 ships binary_treatment=True with a control-function logit likelihood for exactly this case, but src/cmp/estimators.py:399-407 never passes it), on a FAST run (2 chains, 400 draws) whose committed stderr shows unaddressed r-hat greater than 1.01 and ESS below 100 warnings. P(pays)=1.00 inherits the overconfidence (honest number about 0.99, so the BUY call survives - but that must be shown, not assumed). The LATE-vs-ATE framing is words-only: the DGP effect is homogeneous, so LATE equals ATE by construction and the validate step quietly benchmarks the 'not-the-ATE' estimator against the ATE without saying why that is legitimate. Validation is single-seed with no coverage or multi-seed check; the euro decision is a single fixed cost with no break-even sweep despite the opener promising budgets and frequency caps. Kernel (cmp-legacy), the cores=1 workaround (confirmed by 'Sequential sampling (2 chains in 1 job)' in the committed stderr and estimators.py:406), seeds/FAST handling, and the README's diagnostic claims (first-stage F, reduced-form/Wald comparison, exclusion stress test) all check out.

**Strengths.**
- The OLS -> reduced-form -> first-stage -> Wald ladder (cells 5-6) is exemplary IV pedagogy: every ingredient is printed transparently (23.7 vs 3.5 / 0.21 = 16.5), the ratio intuition is stated correctly, and the Wald/2SLS logic is exactly right for a single binary randomized instrument - ideal for an MBA audience.
- The exclusion-restriction stress test (cell 11) is a genuine range-based sensitivity analysis with correct algebra: corrected = (reduced - delta)/first, and the honest verdict that the BUY call survives until the instrument's direct effect reaches delta of about 1.4 euros (40 percent of the 3.5-euro reduced form) is exactly the kind of robustness statement the four-axes bar asks for.
- Identification hygiene is strong: relevance/exclusion/exogeneity each labelled testable vs untestable, monotonicity (no defiers) named with the Imbens-Angrist citation, and the final recommendation is correctly LATE-scoped ('BUY exposure (to the complier margin)') rather than overclaiming an ATE.

<details><summary>Axis notes</summary>

- **Diagnostics (4/5).** First-stage F correctly computed (squared t-stat form, Stock-Yogo rule cited) and interpreted; the OLS/reduced/Wald cross-check and the delta-sensitivity are real diagnostics - but no posterior predictive check on the joint model and the advertised endogeneity rho is never actually inspected.
- **Business (3/5).** Clear euro conversion and a correctly LATE-scoped BUY call, but a single fixed cost (10 euros), a saturated P(pays)=1.00, and no break-even cost curve or frequency-cap sweep despite the opener promising both budgets and caps - below the repo's own sweep/break-even bar.
- **Narration (4/5).** Endogeneity, instrument, LATE, compliers, monotonicity all defined on first use with worked Wald math and accurate cross-links (nb 03 cost shifter, nb 05 adjustment limits); held back from 5 by the unexplained homogeneous-effect LATE=ATE seam and the un-shown rho.
- **Validation (2/5).** Single seed, point-recovery only: the committed 90 percent interval excludes the truth while the text declares success, and there is no multi-seed recovery, no coverage/calibration check, and no acknowledgment of the r-hat/ESS warnings sitting in the same cell's output.
- **Watertight (2/5).** The frequentist half (Wald/2SLS, F, delta algebra) is exactly right, but the headline Bayesian interval is indefensible as committed: misspecified Gaussian likelihood on a binary treatment plus an undisclosed N(2SLS, 1) prior yield a ~3.3x overconfident CI that misses the known truth, and P(pays)=1.00 flows from it.

</details>

- **🔴 CRITICAL · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 6 output and cells 7/9 markdown (PDF p.3); nb text extract lines 163-165, 197-204`
  - **Issue.** The validate step's headline claim is contradicted by its own committed output: the Bayesian IV posterior is reported as 'EUR 16.6 ... 90% CI [EUR 15.5, EUR 17.8]', which EXCLUDES the true effect of EUR 15, yet the markdown asserts the posterior 'lands near the true EUR 15' and declares the bias removed. The interval is also far too narrow: replicating the exact DGP (sigmoid(0.3+1.1Z+0.8g); sales = 50+15D+12g+N(0,6); n=3000, seed 37) gives Wald = 16.55 with heteroskedasticity-robust SE 2.31, i.e. an honest 90% CI of [12.7, 20.3], and across 200 seeds the Wald sampling SD is 2.34. The committed credible interval (half-width ~1.15) is ~3.3x overconfident.
  - **Evidence.** Committed output: 'Bayesian IV EUR 16.6 (true EUR 15) 90% CI [EUR 15.5, EUR 17.8]'. Numpy replication of dgp.iv_ad_exposure (src/cmp/dgp.py:394-408): seed37 wald=16.55, robustSE=2.31, 90%CI=[12.7,20.3]; 200 seeds: Wald mean=15.01, sd=2.34, 5-95% range [10.6, 18.7]. The PDF p.3 left panel visibly shows the dashed truth line at the extreme left edge of the posterior mass.
  - **Why it matters.** This is the recover-the-truth step of a truth-recovery notebook; a sharp audience member will immediately see a 90% interval that misses the planted truth in the very figure captioned 'IV removes self-selection bias'. Every downstream euro number (P(pays)=1.00, net 6.6) inherits the overconfidence.

Never claim 'lands near the truth' next to an interval that excludes it.
  - **Verifier.** Factual accuracy verified directly: the notebook extract (cell 6 output, line 165) and PDF p.3 both show 'Bayesian IV €16.6 (true €15) 90% CI [€15.5, €17.8]' — an interval excluding the planted truth of 15 — while cells 7 and 9 (lines 169-172, 197-204) claim the posterior 'lands near the true €15' and that the bias is removed; the PDF figure shows the dashed truth line at the extreme left edge of the posterior mass, as the reviewer says. Statistical soundness verified by independent replication of the exact DGP (src/cmp/dgp.py:394-408): seed 37 gives Wald=16.547 with HC0-robust SE 2.311 and 90% CI [12.75, 20.35], and 200 seeds give Wald mean 15.01, SD 2.34, 5-95% [10.6, 18.7] — all matching …

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:399-407 (est.iv) and cell 4 markdown; confirmed by committed stderr 'NUTS: [beta_t, beta_z, chol_cov]' (extract line 148)`
  - **Issue.** The wrapper fits CausalPy's continuous-treatment model - a bivariate MvNormal likelihood over (sales, exposure) - to a binary 0/1 exposure. causalpy 0.8.1 provides binary_treatment=True (a logit control-function likelihood built precisely for binary treatments, pymc_models.py:771-812), but cmp.estimators.iv never passes it, so the Gaussian branch runs (the sampled parameter list [beta_t, beta_z, chol_cov] in the committed stderr proves this). The Normal first-stage density on 0/1 data is misspecified, which both distorts the meaning of the 'endogeneity rho' the notebook advertises and contributes to the spuriously tight posterior.
  - **Evidence.** .venv-legacy/.../causalpy/pymc_models.py lines 684 and 771-812 (binary_treatment branch with logit link and control-function rho); causalpy/experiments/instrumental_variable.py:128 (binary_treatment=False default); src/cmp/estimators.py:406 passes only sample_kwargs. Committed stderr shows chol_cov sampled, i.e. the MvNormal branch.
  - **Why it matters.** The notebook's stated selling point of the Bayesian IV over the Wald ratio is 'a joint model ... estimating the endogeneity rho rather than assuming it away'; with a Gaussian likelihood on a Bernoulli treatment that joint model is the wrong likelihood, and its extra 'precision' over 2SLS is an artifact, not information.
  - **Fix.** Pass binary_treatment=True in est.iv for this notebook (and surface it as an argument), or explicitly discuss in Step 4 that the Gaussian joint model treats the binary exposure as a linear-probability first stage and that its posterior width should not be taken at face value; add a posterior predictive check that would expose the misspecification.
  - **Verifier.** Factually accurate on every citation: (1) est.iv (src/cmp/estimators.py:399-407) passes only formulas/data/sample_kwargs to cp.InstrumentalVariable and never sets binary_treatment; (2) the installed causalpy 0.8.1 (.venv-legacy) has binary_treatment (default False, experiments/instrumental_variable.py:128) with a logit control-function branch in pymc_models.py (~684, 771-812) whose NUTS parameters would be [beta_t, beta_z, sigma_U, rho_unconstr, u]; the committed stderr 'NUTS: [beta_t, beta_z, chol_cov]' (extract line 148) proves the MvNormal continuous-treatment branch ran, since chol_cov (LKJCholeskyCov) exists only there; (3) dgp.iv_ad_exposure (src/cmp/dgp.py:394-408) generates a strictl…
  - **Corrected statement.** Finding stands as written. One refinement for the fix: the spuriously tight posterior is driven jointly by (a) the misspecified Gaussian joint likelihood on the binary exposure and (b) causalpy's default empirically-centered prior N(2SLS estimate, sigma=1) on the outcome coefficients (and the committed run is also under-sampled: rhat>1.01, ESS<100). Passing binary_treatment=True alone will not restore honest width unless the informative default priors are also widened or the posterior/2SLS width discrepancy is discussed; the correct 2SLS 90% CI on this seed is about [12.8, 20.3] versus the committed [15.5, 17.8].

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `causalpy/experiments/instrumental_variable.py:171-190 (default priors) vs cell 5 markdown claim 'the same idea fit jointly with uncertainty' (extract lines 117-120)`
  - **Issue.** When no priors are given, CausalPy centers the beta priors at the 2SLS point estimates with sd = 1: the exposure coefficient prior is effectively N(16.5, 1). The notebook never discloses this, yet presents the posterior as data-driven uncertainty. A prior whose 90% width (3.3 euros) is less than half the true sampling uncertainty (SD 2.3) means the reported credible interval is substantially manufactured by the default prior, not learned from the data.
  - **Evidence.** instrumental_variable.py algorithm(): priors = {'mus': [ols_beta_first_params, ols_beta_second_params], 'sigmas': [1, 1], ...} where ols_beta_second_params comes from get_2SLS_fit(); docstring line 55: 'If priors are not specified we will substitute MLE estimates'. est.iv (estimators.py:399-407) passes no priors.
  - **Why it matters.** For a lecture whose bar is 'watertight statistics', selling a prior-dominated interval as 'the Bayesian version of the Wald ratio with uncertainty' is exactly the kind of claim a sharp Bayesian in the audience would dismantle; it also makes the Bayesian-vs-Wald comparison circular, since the prior is centered on the 2SLS answer.
  - **Fix.** Set explicit weakly-informative priors in est.iv (e.g. sigmas of 10-20 on the euro scale) and say so in the notebook, or keep the defaults but add one honest sentence ('CausalPy centers its default priors on the 2SLS fit with sd 1, so the posterior width understates uncertainty; the robust 2SLS SE is ~2.3'). Print the 2SLS SE next to the Bayesian interval either way.
  - **Verifier.** Every factual citation checks out and the statistical criticism is quantitatively correct. (a) Facts: causalpy 0.8.1's InstrumentalVariable.algorithm() (instrumental_variable.py lines 174-190 in .venv-legacy) defaults priors to mus=[first-stage betas, 2SLS betas], sigmas=[1,1] when none are given; docstring line 55 says verbatim 'If priors are not specified we will substitute MLE estimates'; the cookbook wrapper est.iv (src/cmp/estimators.py:399-407) passes no priors and exposes no way to; the PyMC model (pymc_models.py, InstrumentalVariableRegression.build_model) sets beta_z ~ Normal(2SLS betas, 1) on RAW data (no standardization), so sd=1 means 1 euro; notebook cell 5 (extract lines 117-12…

  - **Issue.** The committed run shows explicit sampler warnings - 'The rhat statistic is larger than 1.01 for some parameters. - **🟠 MAJOR · pedagogical** — `Cells 0, 4, 7 (LATE framing) vs src/cmp/dgp.py:405 (homogeneous effect); README line 60 'LATE-vs-ATE framing'`
  - **Issue.** The notebook teaches emphatically that 'IV does not give the average effect over everyone' - then simulates a DGP where the effect is exactly 15 euros for every customer (sales = 50 + true_effect*ad_exposure + ...), so LATE = ATE by construction, and Step 5 validates the IV estimate against the average truth without ever noting why that comparison is legitimate. The complier share, which is just the first stage (0.21) under monotonicity, is printed but never named as such.
  - **Evidence.** dgp.py:405 uses a constant true_effect with no interaction; cell 0: 'IV does not give the average effect over everyone'; cell 7 validates against true_effect=15; cell 3 prints 'shift +21%' without identifying it as the complier share.
  - **Why it matters.** A sharp audience member can object: 'you just told me IV is not the ATE, then you graded it against the ATE.' The single most distinctive lesson of IV - that it answers a different question for a specific subpopulation - is asserted but never demonstrated, so the README's 'LATE-vs-ATE framing' claim is delivered in words only.
  - **Fix.** Either (a) add effect heterogeneity correlated with compliance to the DGP (e.g. effect = 15 + 8*engagement), compute the true complier-weighted LATE and show IV recovering IT and not the ATE - a far stronger lecture beat - or (b) at minimum state 'in this simulation the effect is homogeneous, so LATE = ATE = 15 and the comparison below is fair; in general they differ', and label the 0.21 first stage as the estimated complier share.

- **🟠 MAJOR · business** — `Cell 10-11 (Decide step) vs the opening promise in cell 0`
  - **Issue.** The euro decision is a single fixed COST = 10 with a saturated P(pays) = 1.00: no break-even cost ('exposure pays as long as an impression costs less than X at 90% confidence'), no cost or effect sweep, no value-of-information, and the frequency-cap decision promised in the opening ('set budgets and frequency caps that don't overspend') is never quantified - the only cap content is one rhetorical sentence in the how-to-read cell.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 11 contains only net = iv_post - COST and the delta-sweep; no sweep over COST; opening line 14 promises 'budgets and frequency caps'; README lines 62-65 set the sweep/break-even bar.
  - **Why it matters.** Against the repo's own business-richness bar (multi-parameter sweeps, break-even tables, policy comparisons), this is the thinnest Decide step reviewed relative to what the intro promises; a P=1.00 no-tension decision also teaches nothing about using the posterior for a call.
  - **Fix.** Add a break-even curve P(effect > c) over a grid of costs c (the posterior makes this one line), state the maximum viable cost-per-exposure at the 90% threshold, and either add a simple diminishing-returns frequency illustration or drop 'frequency caps' from the opening.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cells 4 and 5 markdown ('estimating the endogeneity rho rather than assuming it away', stated twice)`
  - **Issue.** The endogeneity correlation rho is advertised twice as the Bayesian model's distinctive feature but is never extracted, printed, or plotted; the fitted chol_cov sits unused in the idata.
  - **Evidence.** No cell touches result.idata.posterior['chol_cov'] or a derived correlation; the only extraction is beta_z (cell 6).
  - **Why it matters.** It is the one diagnostic unique to the Bayesian IV over the Wald ratio, and showing rho clearly positive would directly quantify the self-selection the notebook is about - a missed, nearly free payoff (and repeating the claim without the number invites the question).
  - **Fix.** Derive the implied error correlation from chol_cov (posterior of cov[0,1]/sqrt(cov[0,0]*cov[1,1])), print its posterior interval, and connect it to the OLS-minus-IV gap.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cell 6 print ('90% CI') and cell 3 print ('shift +21%')`
  - **Issue.** Two language slips: the posterior interval is labelled '90% CI' rather than credible interval (in a notebook whose bar is precise posterior language), and the first-stage shift '+21%' is percentage points (56% to 77%), not a 21 percent relative change.
  - **Evidence.** Extract line 139 f-string '90% CI [...]'; line 76 format '{fs[1]-fs[0]:+.0%}' rendering '+21%'.
  - **Why it matters.** Both are the kind of terminology imprecision the repo's own quality bar says a sharp audience member could poke at, and pp-vs-percent confusion is a classic MBA trap.
  - **Fix.** Print '90% credible interval' (or 'CrI') and 'shift +21 pp'.

- **⚪ POLISH · consistency** — `Cell 0 last line ('7-step contract.') and cell 8 legend rounding (PDF pp.2-3)`
  - **Issue.** The dangling line '7-step contract.' renders as an orphaned body-text sentence in the PDF (p.2), and the validation figure legend rounds to whole euros ('OLS EUR 24', 'Wald EUR 17' vs text values 23.7 and 16.5), which makes 'Wald EUR 17' sit awkwardly next to 'true EUR 15' in the very panel claiming the Wald lands near the truth.

  - **Fix.** Turn the orphan line into the standard contract blurb or delete it; use one decimal in the legend labels.

---

### Anchor A — adversarial statistics lens

**Grade: adequate** · 3 critical · 4 major · 14 findings total

The notebook's core machinery is largely sound — the AIPW score formula, the truth-graded Qini/AUUC construction, the uplift-by-decile logic, the P(tau>cost) targeting rule, the overlap diagnostic, and the pymc-bart sample_vars counterfactual fix all check out against the source. But the flagship is not watertight where it matters most for a hostile audience: the headline E-value (5.76) is computed on the TRUE simulated ATE (not the estimate) via a ratio mapping that is not a risk ratio in any sense — the code's own recommended continuous-outcome conversion gives ~1.5, a near-4x overstatement of robustness; the Depth A read-out flatly contradicts its own printed bake-off table (T-learner beats BCF on PEHE, corr, and coverage); 90% intervals cover only 76% overall and near-0% in the top true-effect decile (the very customers being targeted) with no comment, while the 'honest rule' and the VOI are built on that same overconfident posterior; the 'posterior predictive check' is not drawn from the fitted model and cannot fail; and the sensitivity contour is an invented arithmetic surface labelled as 'apparent ATE'. These are exactly the places a sharp referee would strike.

**Strengths.**
- AIPW score is textbook-correct (metrics: mu1-mu0 + T(y-mu1)/e - (1-T)(y-mu0)/(1-e), estimators.py:167-169) and the bootstrap re-runs both nuisance fits inside every resample (aipw_ate._score), so the CI honestly propagates nuisance-estimation noise.
- Qini/AUUC internals are coherent: random baseline frac*sum(tau), oracle curve from sorted true tau, AUUC normalised as area-above-random over oracle-area-above-random (metrics.py:45-72) — the printed ‘0.90 of oracle’ means exactly what the text says it means, and uplift-by-decile (metrics.py:75-91) is computed on the true effect within predicted deciles as claimed.
- The sample_vars=["mu"] counterfactual gotcha is real, correctly handled in _bart_predict (estimators.py:59-73), asserted by a unit test, and honestly narrated — a genuinely valuable trap to teach.
- The overlap check is not decorative: the DGP’s true propensity index is linear in the observed features (dgp.py:57), so the logistic e(x) is essentially correctly specified, and the ‘0 units in the <0.05/>0.95 tails’ claim is consistent with the DGP’s actual propensity range (~[0.05, 0.95]).
- Identification is stated precisely and kept separate from estimation: {Y(0),Y(1)} ⊥ T | X, positivity as 0<e(x)<1, SUTVA, backdoor/g-formula — and the credible-interval gloss in cell 0 is the correct Bayesian statement, not a confidence-interval confusion.
- The euro-policy arithmetic is right: profit_curve, cost_sweep, confidence_sweep and policy_comparison (policy.py) all realise profit as sum(tau_true - cost) over the chosen mask, matching the printed €-1,734 / €2,681 / €3,058 figures, and the notebook is explicit that truth-graded evaluation is a simulation-only privilege.
- The naive difference-of-means demonstration (€11.6 vs true €6.1, cell 24) is arithmetically and conceptually correct, and the S-learner failure demo is fair on the point-estimate axis: same BART family and same BPROF budget as the T-learner, so the flattening (corr 0.54-0.58 vs 0.80-0.84) reflects the learner, not rigged compute.
- The Depth B tipping-point sweep (left panel) is a real computation: data are regenerated at each confounder strength and re-adjusted blind to U, exactly as the text describes.

- **🔴 CRITICAL · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 28 (evalue = metrics.e_value(tau.mean(), COST)) and src/cmp/metrics.py:113-130`
  - **Issue.** The headline E-value of 5.76 is statistically indefensible twice over: (1) it is computed on the TRUE simulated ATE tau.mean()=6.07, not on any estimate — leaking the simulation truth into a robustness claim that is supposed to model what a real analyst could do; (2) with sd omitted, e_value uses the fallback rr = |estimate|/|estimate-cost| = 6.07/1.93 = 3.15 and plugs that into E = RR + sqrt(RR(RR-1)). A ratio of an additive €-effect to its gap from a €-threshold is not a risk ratio on any scale; VanderWeele's E-value formula is only defined for risk ratios. The function's own documented, recommended path for continuous outcomes (RR ≈ exp(0.91·d)) gives d = 1.93/sd_y ≈ 0.10-0.16, hence E ≈ 1.4-1.6 — the notebook overstates robustness by nearly 4x.
  - **Evidence.** metrics.py:128: rr = max(abs(estimate)/max(abs(estimate-cost),1e-6), 1+1e-6) — verified 6.07/1.93=3.152, E=5.756 matches the printed 5.76. With the function's own exp(0.91d) branch and sd(y)≈12-20 (DGP noise σ=8 plus baseline spread), E ≈ 1.41-1.58. Cell 28 passes tau.mean() (the planted truth), not the BCF (6.57) or AIPW (5.85) estimate.
  - **Why it matters.** The one-paragraph decision (cell 31) leans on this number ('would only reverse under fairly strong hidden confounding'). A referee who knows VanderWeele-Ding will compute the correct E-value (~1.5, i.e. quite fragile) in their head and the credibility of the whole Depth B section collapses. It also sits oddly next to the same cell's tipping point of ~1.5 confounder-strength units, which tells a fragility story.
  - **Fix.** Call metrics.e_value(aipw['ate'] or cate_bcf.mean(), COST, sd=y.std()) so the standardized-difference conversion is used on an *estimated* effect; report the honest ~1.5 and rewrite the narrative (the finding is genuinely sensitivity-fragile, which is itself a better teaching point). Remove or clearly deprecate the ratio fallback in metrics.e_value.
  - **Verifier.** Both prongs verified by direct reading. (a) Factually accurate: cell 28 of notebooks/02... — i.e., the 01_uplift_targeting extract line 946 — calls metrics.e_value(tau.mean(), COST) where tau is documented in cell 3 as "the PLANTED true effect (known only in sim)" (true_ate=6.0732 in cell 32, vs bcf_ate=6.566 and aipw_ate=5.852, matching the reviewer's cited 6.57/5.85). With sd omitted, src/cmp/metrics.py:128 computes rr=6.0732/1.9268=3.1520 and line 130 gives E=3.152+sqrt(3.152·2.152)=5.7564, exactly the printed 5.756405386243919. Cell 31's decision paragraph explicitly leans on this E-value. (b) Statistically sound: the VanderWeele–Ding formula E=RR+sqrt(RR(RR−1)) is defined only for risk …
  - **Corrected statement.** The finding stands as written. If anything it understates the problem: besides (1) computing the E-value on the planted truth tau.mean()=6.07 rather than an estimate and (2) feeding a non-risk-ratio quantity (6.07/1.93=3.15) into the VanderWeele–Ding formula to get the reported 5.76 where the function's own recommended exp(0.91·d) conversion with sd(y)≈15 gives E≈1.5, note also that (3) tau.mean()=6.07 is already below the €8 break-even, so the "explain the effect down to break-even" framing is directionally incoherent — the |estimate−cost| absolute value silently hides that the shift being priced runs upward, not downward.

- **🔴 CRITICAL · consistency — ✅ verified** — `Cell 26 (markdown read-out) vs cell 24 output table`

Per src/cmp/metrics.py, PEHE is RMSE of posterior-mean CATE vs truth ("per-customer recovery") and cov90 is empirical coverage of 90% credible intervals over ~5,000 units, so 0.76 is a large, statistically unambiguous undercoverage — not near-nominal. All three claims in the sentence are contradicted by the table two cells earlier; …
  - **Corrected statement.** In the observational bake-off as printed, the T-learner actually achieves the best per-customer recovery (PEHE 5.85 vs BCF 6.34), equal-or-better ranking (corr 0.84 vs 0.83, AUUC tied at 0.90), and far better interval coverage (0.93 vs 0.76 at nominal 90%); BCF's genuine advantages in this table are the smallest ATE bias (0.49 vs 1.00) and agreement with the independent AIPW estimate. The read-out should say this, noting that with fully observed, well-overlapped confounders BCF ≈ T-learner and BCF's edge appears under targeted selection / regularization-induced confounding — or the DGP should be strengthened so BCF demonstrably wins before the claim is made.

- **🔴 CRITICAL · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 15 output ('90% coverage 76%') and the coverage-by-decile figure (PDF p.7, middle panel); downstream cells 19, 29-30`
  - **Issue.** The model's 90% credible intervals cover the truth only 76% of the time overall, and the notebook's own coverage-by-decile bar chart shows coverage collapsing to near zero in the top true-effect decile — precisely the customers the targeting policy selects. Neither number is ever discussed; the diagnostic was run, plotted, and its alarming verdict ignored. Every downstream probability statement — the 'honest rule' P(τ>cost)>0.8, the straddler count (612), and the VOI (€388) — is computed from this same demonstrably overconfident posterior and presented as 'honest'.

Cell 31: '(In FAST mode the posterior is deliberately coarse…)'.
  - **Why it matters.** The notebook's whole pitch is 'sharp AND calibrated, never sharpness alone' (cell 14) and 'honest uncertainty is exactly what the euro decision needs' (cell 26). A posterior that misses the biggest effects ~95% of the time makes P(τ>cost) miscalibrated exactly where it drives the spend decision, understates VOI, and undercuts the A/B-test sizing recommendation. The cell-31 footnote blames FAST mode, but coarse MCMC makes intervals noisy, not systematically too narrow — undercoverage persists in the bake-off (BCF 0.76-0.82 in both regimes), so it is a model property (shrinkage from the tau-forest prior), not a sampling artifact.
  - **Fix.** Add an explicit reading of the coverage-by-decile figure: BCF's shrinkage prior under-covers the extreme effects; quantify it, and either (a) widen/calibrate intervals (e.g. conformalize the CATE intervals or loosen the tau-forest prior) before running the policy step, or (b) carry the miscalibration into the decision narrative ('P>0.8 is nominal, true confidence is lower; the straddler set is a lower bound'). At minimum delete the claim that the FAST switch explains it.
  - **Verifier.** Every cited fact checks out against the artifacts. (a) Factual accuracy: cell 15 prints "90% coverage 76% · interval sharpness €13.6" (nb_text line 400); cell 32 JSON has coverage90=0.764; PDF p.7 middle panel shows the top true-effect decile bar at ~0.05 and decile 0 at ~0.45 against the 0.9 dashed line; the reliability panel shows the top bin's true effect ~27 vs predicted ~17 (corroborated by cell 16's own "top decile true effect €27.6" vs the ~€17 predicted-CATE line on PDF p.8). - **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 13 (PPC construction) and the 'Posterior predictive check' figure (PDF p.6)`
  - **Issue.** The 'posterior predictive check' is not drawn from the fitted model. cond_mean = (y.mean() - cate_point.mean()*T.mean()) + cate_point*T replaces the fitted prognostic surface prog(x) with a single grand-mean constant, then sets the replicate noise to the sd of the resulting residuals (€15) — which absorbs ALL baseline heterogeneity (the DGP's true noise is σ=8, and BCF actually fits prog(x)). The replicate envelope is therefore roughly twice as wide as the model's real predictive distribution, so the marginal histogram of y can essentially never fall outside it: the check is vacuous by construction.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 13 code: resid_sd = std(y - cond_mean) with cond_mean ignoring x entirely; printed 'residual sigma ~ €15' vs dgp.py:68 noise = rng.normal(0, 8, n). est.bcf (estimators.py:136) returns only tau samples, so the notebook had no prog(x) draws to build a genuine PPC from.
  - **Why it matters.** It is presented as 'model criticism' gating whether we may read effects off the model ('if the real data looked nothing like what the model can generate…'). A referee will note that (a) no quantity from the fitted posterior is used except the CATE point estimate, (b) a check that cannot fail is not a check, and (c) the marginal-histogram PPC would not detect conditional misfit anyway.
  - **Fix.** Have est.bcf optionally return posterior draws of mu = prog + tau*z (it is already a Deterministic in the model) and build y_rep = Normal(mu_draws, sd_draws) — a true PPC with the model's own σ≈8; or use pm.sample_posterior_predictive on y_obs. If only the marginal check is kept, say explicitly it is a weak necessary-condition check.
  - **Verifier.** Both prongs hold. (a) Factually accurate: Cell 13 of 01_uplift_targeting builds cond_mean = (y.mean() - cate_point.mean()*T.mean()) + cate_point*T — a single grand-mean constant plus tau*T, ignoring x — then sets replicate noise to std(y - cond_mean), printed as €15. dgp.py:68 confirms true noise sigma=8; the €15 is quantitatively explained by absorbed baseline heterogeneity (sd(mu0) ≈ 12.9 from the mu0 formula at dgp.py:49; sqrt(165+64) ≈ 15.1). estimators.py:127-136 confirms the BCF model does fit prog(x) and mu = Deterministic("mu", prog + tau_b*z) with fitted HalfNormal sd, but bcf() returns only idata.posterior["tau"], so the notebook had no posterior draws of mu/sd to build a real PPC.…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 28, right panel: approx = tau.mean() + G * Dd * 0.9`
  - **Issue.** The sensitivity contour labelled 'apparent ATE (€)' / 'Where the decision flips (black = cost line)' is an invented arithmetic surface: true ATE plus the product of the two axis variables times a hard-coded 0.9. It also starts from the true ATE rather than an estimate.
