  - **Why it matters.** It is displayed side-by-side with a genuine simulation sweep and visually carries the same authority; a referee who asks 'what model produced this contour?' gets the answer 'none'. Presenting decoration as a bias-contour sensitivity analysis (the Cinelli-Hazlett style plot it imitates) is the kind of thing that discredits the genuinely computed left panel too. Relatedly, the left panel's tipping point is computed with a homogeneous-effect OLS adjustment (adjusted_ate), not the BCF pipeline the decision actually uses — the fragility of a different estimator.
  - **Fix.** Either compute the contour honestly (grid over (gamma, delta), simulate U with those coefficients, re-run the adjusted estimator, plot the realised apparent ATE — the left-panel machinery already does this for one ray), or replace it with a proper OVB/Cinelli-Hazlett bound with defined partial-R2 axes; and run the tipping-point sweep through the same estimator used for the decision.
  - **Verifier.** Factually accurate on every point checked. Cell 28 of 01_uplift_targeting really computes the right-panel contour as `approx = tau.mean() + G * Dd * 0.9` over a bare meshgrid — no data, refit, or model is referenced — and labels it 'apparent ATE (€)' / 'Where the decision flips'. `tau` is the planted true effect (extract line 124), so the surface anchors at the true ATE, not an estimate. In src/cmp/dgp.py (uplift_customers, ~lines 56-71) the hidden confounder enters treatment via sigmoid(lin + 0.9*strength*U) and the outcome additively (y += strength*U), confirming the 0.9 is a borrowed constant, not a derived OVB coefficient. The criticism is also statistically sound: the plane bias = 0.9·γ…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:145-183 (aipw_ate) as invoked in cell 11`
  - **Issue.** The AIPW cross-check is advertised as doubly robust, but the implementation fits the gradient-boosted outcome models and the logistic propensity on the full sample and evaluates them on the same observations — no cross-fitting/sample-splitting. With adaptive learners like GBR, in-sample residuals (y - mu_t) are shrunk by overfitting, which biases the augmentation terms toward zero and voids the conditions under which the AIPW estimator is root-n consistent and its influence-function SE valid (Donsker or cross-fitting required, à la Chernozhukov et al. DML). Additionally the docstring claims 'overlap is enforced by trimming units with propensity outside [trim, 1-trim]' but the code only clips e(x) and drops nothing (n_trim is merely reported). Finally, 'entirely different machinery than BART' overstates independence: both pipelines rest on the same logistic-in-raw-features propensity specification.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:159-169: _score fits m1/m0 on Xi[Ti==1]/[Ti==0] and predicts on all of Xi (same units); no fold structure anywhere. Docstring line 152-153 ('Overlap is enforced by trimming') vs line 162 (np.clip only).
  - **Why it matters.** The cross-check is the notebook's stated credibility anchor ('correct if either one is right'). A methods referee will ask 'where is the cross-fitting?' — and without it the DR guarantee being sold does not formally apply; the bootstrap partially rescues the CI but not the first-order bias from own-observation fitting.
  - **Fix.** Add 2- or 5-fold cross-fitting inside _score (fit nuisances on the complement fold, evaluate out-of-fold) — ~6 lines; fix the docstring to say 'propensities clipped at [trim, 1-trim]'; and soften 'entirely different machinery' to acknowledge the shared propensity specification.
  - **Verifier.** All three factual sub-claims verified by reading src/cmp/estimators.py and the notebook extract. (1) No cross-fitting: _score (estimators.py:159-170) fits the logistic propensity and both GBR outcome models on the full index set and evaluates them on the same observations; grep confirms no fold/KFold/cross-fit structure anywhere in src/cmp. The bootstrap (line 181) repeats the same in-sample fitting per replicate, so it captures variance but replicates, rather than corrects, the own-observation bias — exactly as the reviewer stated. (2) Docstring lines 151-153 claim units are trimmed for overlap, but line 162 only clips e(x) (winsorization) and lines 172-174 compute n_trim purely for reporti…

- **🟠 MAJOR · business** — `Cell 19 output table (comp.round(0)) and cell 18 markdown vs policy.py:117-123`
  - **Issue.** The printed decision table is internally wrong as displayed: comp.round(0) rounds frac_contacted to integers, so 'random-50%' shows frac_contacted = 1.0 and both model rules and the oracle show 0.0 — directly contradicting the next printed line ('targeting 236 of 900 customers', i.e. 0.26). Separately, the markdown promises a 'treat-none (baseline)' row among the evaluated policies, but policy_comparison never includes it (its own docstring lists it too), so the table has no zero baseline.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 19 output: 'random-50% 1414.0 1.0 3148.0' and 'model (P>conf) 2681.0 0.0 4416.0' vs printed 'targeting 236 of 900'. policy.py:117-123 policies dict has no 'treat-none'; docstring line 97 promises it, as does cell 18.
  - **Why it matters.** This is the notebook's money slide — the euro decision step. A table asserting the random policy contacts 100% of the base and the model contacts 0% while the text says 26% would be caught instantly in the lecture and casts doubt on the other columns.
  - **Fix.** Print with per-column rounding (e.g. comp.round({'profit': 0, 'frac_contacted': 2})) and add the treat-none row (profit 0) to policy_comparison so the baseline the text references exists.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cell 29 markdown + policy.py:64-88 (value_of_information)`
  - **Issue.** Two slippages in the VOI story. (1) The per-customer VOI is EVPI relative to the 'act on the posterior mean' policy (max(E[τ]-c, 0)), but the policy the notebook actually recommends is P(τ>cost)>0.8 — VOI measured against the deployed rule would be larger, so €388 understates what resolving uncertainty is worth to *this* decision-maker. (2) 'The total VOI on the straddlers is, quite literally, the maximum you'd rationally pay for a clean experiment on them' conflates EVPI with EVSI: an A/B test delivers sample information, worth strictly less than perfect information, so EVPI is an upper bound, not the literal willingness-to-pay for a finite experiment.
  - **Evidence.** policy.py:78: ev_act_on_mean = np.maximum(mean_cate - cost, 0) while the recommended mask is p_worth > 0.8 (policy.py:31-32); cell 29: 'quite literally, the maximum you'd rationally pay for a clean experiment on them'.
  - **Why it matters.** VOI is used to size and justify the follow-up experiment; a decision-theory-literate audience member will note both the mismatched baseline policy and the EVPI/EVSI conflation, and the €388 also inherits the demonstrated posterior overconfidence (finding 3).
  - **Fix.** Compute VOI against the actually-recommended policy (E[(τ-c)·1{P>0.8}] baseline) or state the act-on-mean convention; replace 'quite literally the maximum you'd pay for a clean experiment' with 'an upper bound on what any experiment on them can be worth (perfect-information value)'.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cells 14/16 ('Qini / uplift curve') and metrics.py:45-58; cell 21 (real-data path)`
  - **Issue.** What is computed is a truth-graded uplift curve (cumulative true τ by predicted rank), not Radcliffe's Qini, which is defined from *observed* outcomes (cumulative treated responses minus rescaled control responses). Calling it 'Qini' is a mislabel a specialist will flag. More substantively: the observed-outcome Qini is the only ranking diagnostic available in production, and the real-data cell (Hillstrom, an RCT where it is straightforwardly computable) computes no ranking diagnostic at all — so the notebook never demonstrates the version of the tool practitioners would actually use.
  - **Evidence.** metrics.py:54-57 uses tau_true directly (cum_model = cumsum(tau_true[order])); cell 21 computes only naive_ate and a P(effect>0) share — no observed-outcome Qini/AUUC on the RCT.
  - **Why it matters.** The lecture's bridge from simulation to 'the money is made here' loses its key evaluation instrument exactly at the hand-off; an uplift-modeling practitioner in the audience will notice both the naming and the gap.
  - **Fix.** Rename to 'oracle-graded uplift curve' in the text (one clause: 'the observable Qini replaces true τ with observed outcomes reweighted by arm'), add an observed-outcome qini function to metrics, and call it in the Hillstrom cell.

- **🟡 MINOR · code** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:89-91 (s_learner counterfactual scoring)`
  - **Issue.** The S-learner's two counterfactual scores are drawn by two *independent* posterior-predictive calls (seeds seed+90 and seed+91) on the same fitted model. Because f(x,1) and f(x,0) come from the same posterior, their draws are strongly positively correlated and the shared-baseline noise should cancel in the difference; scoring them independently breaks that correlation and inflates the S-learner's CATE posterior variance beyond the model's actual posterior. The point estimate (and hence the flattening/PEHE story) is essentially unaffected, but the S-learner's interval-based metrics (cov90 = 0.72-0.73) partly reflect implementation rather than the learner.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:91: return _bart_predict(model, idata, X1, seed+90, ...) - _bart_predict(model, idata, X0, seed+91, ...) — two separate sample_posterior_predictive calls with different seeds on one shared posterior. (For the T-learner the two models are genuinely independent, so there it is correct.)
  - **Why it matters.** The bake-off's coverage column is used to indict the S-learner as 'mis-calibrated'; a careful reader of estimators.py can attribute part of that verdict to decorrelated counterfactual draws, slightly weakening the failure-mode demo's fairness.
  - **Fix.** Score X1 and X0 in a single sample_posterior_predictive call (stack [X1; X0], split the output) so each posterior draw's tree ensemble is applied to both counterfactuals, preserving within-draw correlation.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cell 24 (bake-off design) and estimators.py:105-136 (bcf)`
  - **Issue.** Two fairness/claim wrinkles in the bake-off. (1) Unequal compute: the observational BCF row reuses cate_bcf fitted with PROF (140 draws, m=30 prognostic trees) while the S/T-learners in the same table use BPROF (90 draws, m=25). (2) The narration says BCF 'puts a tighter prior on the treatment part'; in the implementation the only tightening is fewer trees (m//2), and the tau-BART is initialized with the raw outcome y (estimators.py:128), so its leaf-scale prior is set by the full outcome spread, not by an effect-scale prior as in Hahn-Murray-Carvalho — the shrink-to-homogeneity claim overstates what this simplified BCF does (consistent with its observed under-coverage).
  - **Evidence.** Cell 24: run('observational','BCF', cate_bcf, tau) reuses the PROF fit from cell 11; BPROF = dict(draws=90, tune=90, m=25). estimators.py:122 m_tau = max(10, m//2); line 128 tau_b = pmb.BART('tau', X=Xtd, Y=y, m=m_tau).
  - **Why it matters.** A referee comparing rows will ask whether BCF's numbers benefited from 55% more draws and more trees; and the 'tighter prior' language attributes a specific published mechanism the code only partially implements.
  - **Fix.** Refit BCF with BPROF for the bake-off row; soften the text to 'fewer trees on the treatment surface, a weak form of the BCF shrinkage prior', or implement an actual scale prior on the treatment forest.

- **🟡 MINOR · pedagogical** — `Cell 0 (four-types table) vs cell 3 (types = np.where(tau > COST, 'persuadable', ...))`
  - **Issue.** The 2x2 typology defines a Persuadable behaviourally (τ > 0: the email tips them over) but the code classifies persuadable as τ > COST = €8 and lumps everyone with 0 < τ < 8 into 'sure thing / lost cause'. The printed shares (32% persuadable, 39% sure-thing/lost-cause) therefore do not correspond to the definition the audience was just given, and 'sure thing' now silently includes customers the email genuinely moves (just by less than €8).
  - **Evidence.** Cell 0 table: 'Persuadable — the email tips them over, τ > 0'; cell 3: types = np.where(tau > COST, 'persuadable', np.where(tau < 0, 'sleeping dog', 'sure thing / lost cause')).
  - **Why it matters.** A student cross-checking panel (1) of the tau histogram (mass between 0 and the €8 line) against the type shares will find the definitions inconsistent; conceptually it conflates 'the email changes behaviour' with 'the email is profitable'.
  - **Fix.** Either classify persuadable as τ > 0 and add a separate 'profitable persuadable (τ > cost)' count, or annotate the cell that it uses an economic (τ > cost) rather than behavioural cut.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cells 17 and 24 outputs (stderr) and estimators.py:54,134 (compute_convergence_checks=False)`
  - **Issue.** Committed outputs repeatedly show 'Only 80/90 samples per chain. Reliable r-hat and ESS diagnostics require longer chains', and the estimators globally disable convergence checks; no r-hat/ESS is reported anywhere in the notebook. So every posterior-derived quantity (P(τ>cost), interval widths, VOI, straddler counts) rests on chains whose convergence is asserted, never shown — and the committed FAST-mode figures (e.g. the reliability curve and coverage bars) are built from 280 total draws.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 17/24 stderr blocks (repeated 8+ times); estimators.py:54 and 134 pass compute_convergence_checks=False; PROF FAST = 140 draws x 2 chains.
  - **Why it matters.** For a notebook whose pitch is honest Bayesian uncertainty, visible unaddressed sampler warnings in the committed output are exactly what the review brief flags: a sharp attendee will ask 'did the chains converge?' and there is no answer on the page.
  - **Fix.** Add one diagnostic line after the main BCF fit (az.summary min ESS / max r-hat on tau or sd) with a FAST-mode caveat when thresholds are missed, and regenerate the committed PDF from a full (CMP_FAST=0) run so lecture-grade figures are not built on 280 draws.

- **⚪ POLISH · code** — `Cell 5, panel 3 (rate line) and cell 21 (Hillstrom subsetting)`
  - **Issue.** Two small figure/data hygiene points. (1) The engagement-decile email rates are plotted against np.linspace(engage.min(), engage.max(), 10) instead of the deciles' actual bin centers; for beta(2,3) engagement the quantile bins are not equispaced, so the orange 'P(was emailed)' curve is horizontally misaligned with the data cloud. (2) The gated Hillstrom cell fits BCF on the first 4000 rows (Xr[:4000]) while the loader preserves CSV row order with no shuffle — if the file is ordered on anything, the subsample is non-random (the propensity was also fit on the full n but applied to the slice).
  - **Evidence.** Cell 5: ax[2].plot(np.linspace(df.engage.min(), df.engage.max(), 10), rate.values, ...) after pd.qcut deciles; cell 21: est.bcf(Xr[:4000], Tr[:4000], yr[:4000], phr[:4000], ...); data.py load_hillstrom does a plain read_csv with no shuffle.
  - **Why it matters.** Panel 3 is the notebook's visual definition of the confounding; a misplotted x-axis slightly distorts the shape. The Hillstrom slice would matter the day someone actually sets CMP_REAL=1 in front of the class.
  - **Fix.** Plot rate against the qcut bin midpoints (e.g. groupby(...).agg engage mean); in cell 21 take a seeded random subsample (rng.choice) instead of the head slice.

---

### Anchor B — adversarial statistics lens

**Grade: adequate** · 1 critical · 6 major · 14 findings total

The scaffolding is genuinely sound: the estimand is stated precisely, the simplex constraint is truly enforced (Dirichlet prior exactly; SLSQP with bounds plus equality constraint), the Abadie RMSE-ratio permutation p-value is computed correctly (treated unit included in rank and denominator, 1/30), and there is no leakage of the simulated truth into estimation. But the notebook fails its own headline validation without acknowledging it: the planted true total lift (EUR 339.5k) lies OUTSIDE the reported 90% credible interval [279k, 333k] — visible as the 'true' line in the far tail of the decision histogram — while the narrative claims 'synthetic control lands on the truth.' The mechanism is diagnosable: the posterior band is built from weight-draw uncertainty only (the fitted observation-noise sd is sampled and then discarded), so the counterfactual band understates predictive uncertainty, and the P(lift>cost)=0.62 that drives the TEST FURTHER call comes from this too-narrow, downward-biased distribution; the 'mildly conservative' caveat that rationalizes the shortfall is asserted from a single seed with no multi-seed recovery or coverage evidence. Several supporting analyses have vulnerabilities a hostile referee would catch: the RMSE-ratio placebo loop leaves the treated (post-treatment) unit in every placebo's donor pool (unlike placebo_in_space, which correctly removes it); the leave-one-out check compares SLSQP point refits against a Bayesian baseline so the entire LOO range sits above the baseline; the launch-date sensitivity mixes estimator instability with a mechanical estimand shift and its own figure (15.9 to 13.0 at +3 weeks, about -18%) contradicts the summary claim that 'the estimate barely moves'; go_no_go prints 'expected_roi: 4.99' which is net value in EUR k, not an ROI; and no MCMC diagnostics are reported anywhere, with convergence checks explicitly disabled. Correct permutation machinery, but the uncertainty quantification — the notebook's selling point ('uncertainty for free') — is the weakest link.

**Strengths.**
- The Abadie RMSE-ratio permutation p-value (cell 16) is constructed correctly: the treated unit is included in both the rank and the denominator (rank 1/30, p = 0.0333), the statistic is post/pre RMSE exactly as described, and the markdown definition in cell 15 matches the code line-for-line.
- placebo_in_space (estimators.py:299-322) correctly excludes the treated unit from every placebo's donor pool (others removes both treated_idx and j), uses the finite-sample (count+1)/(N+1) permutation correction, and applies a two-sided comparison via absolute post-gap means.
- The simplex constraint is genuinely enforced in both fitters: the Dirichlet prior lives exactly on the simplex, and sc_weights_slsqp (estimators.py:232-246) imposes bounds (0,1) plus an explicit sum-to-one equality constraint — this is real Abadie-style no-extrapolation, not a rhetorical claim.
- No truth leakage: dgp.geo_panel returns true_effect separately (dgp.py:115-117) and nothing in the estimation path touches it; the factor-loading DGP genuinely makes both naive estimators fail (24.3 and 27.1 vs true 17.0) while keeping the effect identifiable for SC.
- Placebo-in-time (cell 18) is done correctly: the fit is restricted to data truncated at the real launch (y_tr[:launch]), so the fake-launch test cannot be contaminated by actual treatment periods.
- Identification is properly separated from estimation (cell 4), the convex-hull/extrapolation explanation of the simplex is accurate, and the estimand formula matches what the code computes.
- The p_space screening threshold (rmse_multiple=5x pre-RMSE) follows Abadie's recommendation to discard un-matchable placebos, and the complementary RMSE-ratio test correctly avoids any screening, as the text explains.

- **🔴 CRITICAL · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cells 6, 8, 14, 20 outputs; PDF p.5 decision histogram; validation criteria in cell 7`
  - **Issue.** The planted truth is OUTSIDE the reported 90% credible interval, and the notebook never says so: true total = EUR 339.5k vs 90% CI [279.2k, 333.0k], point estimate 305k (a -10% miss). The validation step's own stated criterion ('the estimated total near the planted truth', 'the true per-week effect path should sit inside the posterior band') silently passes, Depth A declares 'synthetic control lands on the truth' (15.2 vs 17.0/week), and the decision histogram (PDF p.5) visibly shows the dashed 'true' line in the far right tail of the posterior.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 6 output: 'estimated total lift EUR 305k (true EUR 339k) . 90% CI [EUR 279k, EUR 333k]'. Cell 20 JSON: true_total 339.48, ci90 [279.23, 332.97]. Cell 14: 'synthetic control lands on the truth.' PDF p.5: 'true' line at 339 sits beyond the histogram bulk.
  - **Why it matters.** This is the anchor notebook's core recover-the-truth check, and it fails on the committed run. A sharp audience member looking at the decision histogram will see the truth outside the 90% interval and ask why the 'validate' section claims success. Worse, the miss directly drives the business call: with an unbiased, correctly-calibrated posterior centered near 339k with this band width, P(lift>300) would be near 0.99 and the decision would be GO, not TEST FURTHER — so the headline decision is an artifact of undercoverage, not of the economics.
  - **Fix.** Acknowledge the non-coverage explicitly in Step 5 and diagnose it: (a) add predictive noise to the counterfactual (see the weight-only-band finding), (b) run a multi-seed recovery loop (e.g. 20 seeds, report bias and 90%-interval coverage of the total), and only then decide whether 'mildly conservative' is bias or one seed's luck. If FAST sampling is partly at fault, regenerate the committed output with the full profile.
  - **Verifier.** Every cited fact verified against the artifacts. (a) Factual: Cell 6 prints "estimated total lift €305k (true €339k) · 90% CI [€279k, €333k]"; Cell 20 JSON has true_total 339.48 vs ci90 [279.23, 332.97] — truth outside the interval, a -10.2% miss. Cell 7 states the recovery criteria exactly as quoted, but Cell 8 computes no coverage check and its output declares success implicitly; Cell 14 claims "synthetic control lands on the truth" (15.2 vs 17.0/week, -11%); caveats mention "mildly conservative" generically but never state the non-coverage. PDF p.5 confirmed visually: the dashed "true" line sits in the far right tail of the decision histogram, and the TRUE lift line escapes the narrow 90%…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:286-289 (synthetic_control) and cells 6, 8, 10`
  - **Issue.** The posterior band and P(lift>cost) are computed from weight-draw uncertainty only: counterfactual_samples = W_post @ donors omits the model's own observation noise — sd is a sampled parameter of the likelihood but is never used when generating the counterfactual, and the treated unit's post-period idiosyncratic noise is likewise unaccounted for. The 'posterior over the counterfactual path' is really a posterior over the fitted mean path.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:286-288: cf = W_post @ donors; effect = target[None,:] - cf — sd appears only in the pre-period likelihood (line 280) and is discarded afterwards. Cell 10 then computes P_value_gt_cost from total_post = effect[:, post].sum(1).
  - **Why it matters.** This is the standard referee objection to naive Bayesian synthetic control: the interval quantifies estimation uncertainty in w but not the predictive uncertainty of the counterfactual, so credible intervals are systematically too narrow (the DGP's noise sd of 3 alone adds roughly sqrt(20)*3 = 13k of sd to the 20-week total, comparable to the entire reported CI half-width of ~27k). It is the proximate cause of the truth falling outside the 90% CI, and it makes P(lift>cost)=0.62 — the number the whole Depth C decision rests on — a probability computed from an indefensibly narrow distribution, despite the notebook selling the method as 'honest uncertainty ... for free'.
  - **Fix.** Build the counterfactual as a posterior predictive: cf = W_post @ donors + rng.normal(0, sd_post[:, None], shape) using the per-draw sd samples (or use pm.sample_posterior_predictive on a model extended over the post period). Re-derive the CI and P(lift>cost) from that, and note in the text that weight-only bands understate uncertainty.
  - **Verifier.** Factually accurate: estimators.py:276-296 samples sd (HalfNormal, line 278) but uses it only in the pre-period likelihood (line 280); lines 286-288 build cf = W_post @ donors and effect = target - cf with no noise term, so the returned 'posterior over the counterfactual path' (docstring line 260) is weight-draw uncertainty only. Cell 6 output confirms truth outside the 90% CI (true €339k vs CI [€279k, €333k]); cell 10 computes P_value_gt_cost = 0.6208 from the same weight-only total_post, driving the Depth C decision; cells 0/5 do claim 'honest uncertainty ... for free'. Statistically sound: the counterfactual Y(0) is a realized path including the treated unit's idiosyncratic noise, so a cor…
  - **Corrected statement.** Finding stands as written. One calibration: correcting the interval would move P(lift>cost) only modestly (~0.62 → ~0.59) because the €300k cost sits near the posterior mean; the decisive symptoms are the CI non-coverage of the truth and the mischaracterized uncertainty, not a sign flip of the decision.

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 16, lines defining ratios (others = np.delete(np.arange(sales.shape[0]), j)) vs src/cmp/estimators.py:311`
  - **Issue.** In the RMSE-ratio placebo loop, each placebo unit's synthetic control is fit on a donor pool that still CONTAINS the actually-treated unit (only j itself is removed), so the treated unit's post-launch +12% lift contaminates every placebo counterfactual. This is inconsistent with placebo_in_space (estimators.py:311), which correctly removes both treated_idx and j.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 16: for j in range(sales.shape[0]): others = np.delete(np.arange(sales.shape[0]), j) — for j != treated_idx, treated_idx remains in others. Contrast estimators.py:311: others = np.delete(np.arange(n), [treated_idx, j]).
  - **Why it matters.** Any weight a placebo synthetic puts on the treated unit inflates that placebo's post-period counterfactual, creating a spurious negative gap and inflating its post/pre RMSE ratio — biasing the treated unit's rank (here conservatively, but the direction is not guaranteed in general, e.g. with negative effects). A referee will note that the notebook's two placebo tests, presented side by side in the same figure, use different donor-pool conventions, and that the canonical Abadie placebo requires the treated unit be excluded from placebo fits precisely because its outcome is contaminated post-treatment.
  - **Fix.** In the ratio loop, drop the treated unit from every placebo's donor pool (others = np.delete(range(n), [treated_idx, j]) for j != treated_idx), keeping the full pool only for the treated unit's own fit — matching placebo_in_space.
  - **Verifier.** Both prongs of the finding hold on direct reading of the artifacts.

(a) Factual accuracy — verified. Cell 16 of the notebook (nb_text/07_geo_lift_synthetic_control.md, lines 394-398) reads exactly as the reviewer quotes: `for j in range(sales.shape[0]): others = np.delete(np.arange(sales.shape[0]), j); gap, _ = est.sc_effect_slsqp(sales[j], sales[others], pre, post)`. Only j itself is removed, so for every placebo j != treated_idx the actually-treated unit (treated_idx, set in Cell ~7 at line 100) remains in the donor pool. By contrast, src/cmp/estimators.py:308-311 (`placebo_in_space`) skips j == treated_idx and uses `others = np.delete(np.arange(n), [treated_idx, j])`, correctly excluding…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 8 (LOO loop and print) and PDF p.5 right panel`
  - **Issue.** The leave-one-out robustness check compares apples to oranges: each LOO refit uses the SLSQP point fitter, but the 'all donors' baseline (305k) is the Bayesian posterior mean. The result is that the ENTIRE LOO range (309-337k) sits above the baseline — a systematic offset from switching estimator (Dirichlet regularization vs exact least-squares), not evidence about donor dependence — yet the print concludes 'no single donor drives it'.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 8: gap, _ = est.sc_effect_slsqp(...) inside the loop vs full_total = eff_mean[post].sum() from the Bayesian effect samples; output 'LOO total range EUR 309-337k around EUR 305k'. PDF p.5: all grey bars at or above the blue 'all donors' line.
  - **Why it matters.** A robustness check whose full-sample reference is generated by a different estimator cannot isolate the effect of dropping a donor. The visible pattern (all six LOO bars on one side of the baseline, with a 28k spread that is as large as the entire 90% CI half-width) would be flagged by a referee as either a donor-dependence signal or an estimator artifact — and as coded, nobody can tell which. Note also dma_10's LOO value (~337k) nearly reaches the truth, hinting that the SLSQP fits are less biased than the Bayesian fit — a diagnostic clue the current setup obscures.
  - **Fix.** Compute the baseline with the same fitter as the LOO refits (est.sc_effect_slsqp on the full donor pool) and report the LOO spread around that; optionally overlay the Bayesian estimate separately. If the SLSQP full-pool total differs materially from 305k, discuss the Dirichlet-shrinkage bias explicitly.
  - **Verifier.** Factual claims verified against the notebook extract and src/cmp/estimators.py: Cell 8's LOO loop uses est.sc_effect_slsqp (exact simplex least-squares, estimators.py:249) while full_total comes from the Bayesian Dirichlet-weight posterior mean (synthetic_control, estimators.py:256), and the print reads 'LOO total range €309–337k around €305k — no single donor drives it.' Statistical soundness verified by deterministic reproduction (seed=3): the same-fitter SLSQP full-pool baseline is €317.7k, not €305k — a ~13k estimator offset (Dirichlet shrinkage) that is ~half the 28k LOO spread. My SLSQP LOO over the top-6 donors reproduces the committed 309.4–337.0k exactly; against the correct 317.7k …
  - **Corrected statement.** The finding stands as written. Additionally, recomputing with the same-fitter baseline (SLSQP full pool = €317.7k) shows LOO deviations of -8.3k to +19.3k: most donors are immaterial, but dropping dma_10 (weight ~0.22) shifts the estimate by +19k toward the truth (€339.5k), so the corrected check would show moderate single-donor sensitivity to dma_10 rather than the notebook's blanket 'no single donor drives it'.

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `Cell 20 (launch-grid loop and left panel of PDF p.9) and cell 21 summary paragraph`
  - **Issue.** The launch-date sensitivity does not measure what the text claims, and its own result contradicts the summary. For L>40 the pre-window includes genuinely treated weeks (contaminated weight fit); for L<40 the reported average g[L:].mean() is mechanically diluted by untreated weeks (true average over weeks 37-59 is 17*20/23 = 14.8, not 17); yet the plot draws the flat true_avg=17 reference for every L. Meanwhile the figure shows the estimate falling from 15.9 (L=40) to 13.0 (L=43), an 18% swing, while the one-paragraph decision claims 'the estimate barely moves when ... the launch date is nudged +/-3 weeks'.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 20: lifts.append(g[L:].mean()) with pre=slice(0,L); ax[0].axhline(true_avg,...) flat at 17.0. PDF p.9 left: points ~13.6, 14.2, 15.0, 15.9, 14.5, 13.9, 13.0 for weeks 37-43. Cell 21: 'the estimate barely moves when any single donor is dropped or the launch date is nudged +/-3 weeks.'
  - **Why it matters.** As designed, the curve conflates three things — estimator instability, contamination of the fitting window, and a change in the estimand's denominator — so it cannot support the stability claim it is invoked for. A sharp audience member comparing the p.9 figure (points spanning 13.0-15.9 against a 17.0 reference) with the sentence 'barely moves' will conclude the summary was written without looking at the plot. In total-lift terms the L=43 point implies roughly EUR 260k, i.e. below the EUR 300k cost — a mis-dated launch would flip the decision, the opposite of the robustness message.
  - **Fix.** Plot the correct L-dependent benchmark (true_effect[L:].mean()) instead of a flat 17.0, restrict the fitting window to min(L, 40) or discuss the contamination for L>40 explicitly, and rewrite the summary sentence to match the observed sensitivity (or report total lift so the decision impact of mis-dating is visible).
  - **Verifier.** Both the factual reading and the statistical criticism check out.

FACTUAL ACCURACY (a): Cell 20 of the notebook extract (nb_text/07_geo_lift_synthetic_control.md, lines 454-462) contains exactly the cited code: `launch_grid = range(launch-3, launch+4)`, `g, _ = est.sc_effect_slsqp(y_tr, donors, slice(0, L), slice(L, W))`, `lifts.append(g[L:].mean())`, and `ax[0].axhline(true_avg, ...)` with `true_avg` computed once in cell 4 as `true_effect[post].mean()` = 17.0 (fixed at the true launch week 40).
