- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/policy.py:171-175 (go_no_go) vs cell 20 (roi = total_post / CAMPAIGN_COST); JSON printed in cell 10 output`
  - **Issue.** go_no_go labels expected net value as ROI: roi = total_effect_samples - cost, reported as 'expected_roi': 4.99 (EUR k of net value). The same notebook then defines ROI = lift/cost in Depth C (ratio ~1.02, P(ROI>1)=0.62). Neither matches the standard ROI = (value-cost)/cost = 0.017. The 4.99 figure is printed verbatim in the decision cell's JSON.
  - **Evidence.** policy.py:171: roi = total_effect_samples - cost; policy.py:175: 'expected_roi': float(roi.mean()) -> 4.991 in cell 10 output. Cell 20: roi = total_post / CAMPAIGN_COST, title 'P(ROI>1) = 0.62'.
  - **Why it matters.** An MBA audience reads 'expected_roi: 4.99' as a 499% return on a campaign that is actually at break-even — the single most misreadable number in the notebook, sitting in the go/no-go output block. Two mutually inconsistent ROI definitions within one notebook (difference in EUR k vs ratio) is exactly the kind of sloppiness that undermines trust in the euro-decision layer.
  - **Fix.** In go_no_go, rename the field to expected_net_value (and/or report expected_roi = (mean - cost)/cost = 0.017). Align the Depth C histogram with the same definition and state it once in the text.
  - **Verifier.** (a) Factually accurate: src/cmp/policy.py:171 is `roi = total_effect_samples - cost` and :175 reports it as `"expected_roi"`; notebook 07 cell 10 prints `"expected_roi": 4.991` (a euro-thousands difference: 304.99 - 300) in the go/no-go JSON; cell 20 defines a second, incompatible ROI as `total_post / CAMPAIGN_COST` (ratio 1.017, title 'P(ROI>1) = 0.62'). (b) Statistically sound: standard ROI = (value - cost)/cost = 0.017 here; labeling a euro difference 'expected_roi' invites an MBA reader to see 4.99 as ~499% return on a break-even campaign, and lift/cost is ROAS, not ROI (break-even at ratio 1 corresponds to ROI = 0). Two mutually inconsistent ROI definitions coexist in one notebook with …

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:281-285 (compute_convergence_checks=False) and cells 6/20 (no r-hat/ESS anywhere)`
  - **Issue.** The Bayesian fit that everything downstream depends on — a 29-dimensional Dirichlet posterior sampled with 2 chains x 600 draws in FAST mode — is run with compute_convergence_checks=False, and the notebook reports no r-hat, no ESS, no divergence count, and no posterior-predictive check of the pre-period fit. The signature MCMC diagnostics for the notebook's own method are entirely absent.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:284: compute_convergence_checks=False; cell 1: FAST=True, chains=2, draws=600; no arviz summary call anywhere in the notebook; sc['idata'] is returned but never inspected.
  - **Why it matters.** The quality bar for this cookbook explicitly requires the method's signature diagnostics, and 'uncertainty for free' from an unverified sampler is not watertight: Dirichlet posteriors over ~29 near-collinear donor series are exactly the geometry where NUTS can mix poorly, and disabling the checks means a pathological committed run would look identical to a healthy one. A referee asking 'what was the minimum ESS on the weights?' gets no answer.
  - **Fix.** Add one diagnostic cell after the fit: az.summary(sc['idata'], var_names=['w','sd']) max r-hat / min bulk-ESS plus divergence count, with a one-line pass/fail read-out; keep compute_convergence_checks on (or at least report the checks) in the full profile, and flag in the text if FAST-mode ESS is below lecture-grade.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cell 16 (est.placebo_in_space(..., sc['pre_rmse'], rmse_multiple=5.0)) and src/cmp/estimators.py:313`
  - **Issue.** The placebo screening threshold mixes fitters and hides its attrition: the reference pre-RMSE is the Bayesian posterior-mean fit (2.86k) while placebo pre-RMSEs come from SLSQP fits (which minimize SSE exactly, hence run smaller), making the 5x gate looser than intended; and the output p_space = 1/21 implies 9 of 29 placebos (31%) were silently discarded, a count never reported, with no sensitivity to the cutoff shown (Abadie recommends reporting 2x/5x/20x).
  - **Evidence.** p_space = 0.047619 = 1/21 exactly (cell 20 JSON), so 20 placebos kept of 29; estimators.py:313 compares SLSQP placebo RMSE against the Bayesian pre_rmse passed in from cell 6.
  - **Why it matters.** Discarding placebos changes both the granularity and the level of the permutation p-value; a reader cannot audit p = 0.048 without knowing the denominator is 21, not 30, and screening that removes a third of the reference distribution deserves a sentence. Since the treated unit is the extreme in all specifications here, the conclusion is robust — but that should be demonstrated, not lucky.
  - **Fix.** Use the treated unit's SLSQP pre-RMSE as the screening reference, print n_kept/n_total from placebo_in_space, and add a one-line table of p_space at rmse_multiple in {2, 5, 20}.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cells 12-14 (Depth A, 'treated - avg ctrl' bake-off)`
  - **Issue.** Naive estimator #2 is computed as a post-period-only level difference, (y_tr[post] - donors[:,post].mean(0)).mean(), so its 27.1 error is dominated by baseline level differences (DMA levels are drawn U(80,140), and dma_00's level happens to sit above the donor mean) — but the markdown attributes the failure to 'the wrong factor loadings'. The explanation does not match the computation, and the estimator most readers would actually reach for (DiD vs the average control, which removes the level gap) is never shown.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 13: naive_avgctrl = (y_tr[post] - donors[:, post].mean(0)).mean() — no pre-period differencing; cell 12: 'the average control has the wrong factor loadings, so it doesn't cancel the common wave cleanly'.
  - **Why it matters.** A sharp audience member will point out that simply demeaning by the pre-period gap fixes most of naive #2, making the bake-off look like a strawman and the stated failure mechanism (loadings, not levels) incorrect as a description of the number on screen. The pedagogical point about loadings is real, but it requires the demeaned comparator to demonstrate it.
  - **Fix.** Add a third naive bar, 'DiD vs avg control' ((post gap) - (pre gap)); this isolates the loadings failure the text describes and makes synthetic control's win honest rather than easy.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cell 4 assumption table, row 'no spillover / interference' ('checked in: design + placebo-in-space')`
  - **Issue.** The table claims placebo-in-space checks the no-spillover assumption, but the placebo test cannot detect spillover: if the campaign contaminated donor markets, the donors (and hence every placebo fit and the treated unit's own synthetic) would be shifted, and nothing in the permutation distribution would flag it. In the simulation, no-spillover holds by construction ('design'), which is the only real check present.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 4 table: 'no spillover / interference ... checked in: design + placebo-in-space (Depth B)'. Depth B (cells 15-16) contains no spillover-sensitive diagnostic.
  - **Why it matters.** This is an identification claim stronger than the diagnostic supports — precisely the failure mode the notebook elsewhere warns against. On real data (the 'On real data' callout encourages exactly that), a user following this table would believe spillover had been tested when it had not; geo spillovers (adjacent DMAs, national media bleed) are the most common real-world SC failure.
  - **Fix.** Change 'checked in' to 'assumed by design (simulation); on real data, exclude geographically adjacent / media-overlapping donors and re-run' — and mention that a donor-pool-restriction robustness check is the practical spillover defence.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `Cells 5, 7, 8 ('pre-RMSE gate ... Step 5 makes it a formal gate'; print 'pre-launch gap mean EUR 0.31k (~0 = good fit)')`
  - **Issue.** The advertised 'formal gate' never materializes: Step 5 prints the mean pre-launch gap (0.31k) with no threshold, no comparison of pre-RMSE (2.86k) to the effect scale or to the donor-pool RMSE distribution, and no pass/fail rule. The mean pre-gap is also a near-mechanical consequence of the least-squares/likelihood fit, so 'approximately 0 = good fit' is close to circular as a check.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 5: '(Step 5 makes it a formal gate)'; cell 8 output: 'pre-launch gap mean EUR 0.31k (~0 = good fit)' — no threshold appears anywhere.
  - **Why it matters.** The notebook (rightly) tells readers that pre-fit quality is THE precondition for interpreting the post gap, then models a check that could not fail. A reader porting this to their own data gets no operational rule for when to abandon the analysis (e.g. pre-RMSE above some fraction of the anticipated effect, or worse than the placebo median).
  - **Fix.** State and apply an explicit gate, e.g. pre-RMSE must be < 1/3 of the minimum detectable weekly effect and below the median placebo pre-RMSE; print PASS/FAIL. Report pre-RMSE relative to the weekly effect (2.86 vs 17.0) rather than the fit-implied mean gap.

- **🟡 MINOR · consistency** — `Cell 21 ('leans on a handful of interpretable donors') vs cell 6 output/figure ('~14.7 effective donors') and cell 5's own caveat`
  - **Issue.** The one-paragraph decision claims the synthetic 'leans on a handful of interpretable donors', directly contradicting the printed metric eff_donors = 14.68 and the figure title 'Synthetic leans on ~14.7 effective donors' — and contradicting the notebook's own earlier caveat that the Dirichlet prior yields a spread, less individually interpretable blend.
  - **Evidence.** Cell 20 JSON: 'eff_donors': 14.68; cell 5: 'a regularized blend spread over many donors ... less individually interpretable'; cell 21: 'leans on a handful of interpretable donors'.
  - **Why it matters.** The executive summary is the one paragraph a VP reads; it asserts the classical-SC sparsity story that the notebook's own diagnostics explicitly disclaim. An attentive reader who saw the 14.7 two pages earlier will distrust the rest of the paragraph.
  - **Fix.** Rewrite to match the output: 'spreads weight over ~15 effective donors (a regularized blend — stable, though less sparse than classical SC)'.

- **🟡 MINOR · code** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:232-246 (sc_weights_slsqp)`
  - **Issue.** The SLSQP fit never checks res.success and never renormalizes: on a silent optimizer failure (maxiter hit, incompatible constraint linearization) the returned x can violate the simplex, and those weights feed directly into the placebo permutation p-values, the LOO check, and the launch-date sensitivity with no warning.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:245-246: res = minimize(...); return res.x — no res.success check, no clipping/renormalization, no fallback.
  - **Why it matters.** The permutation loop runs 29+ SLSQP solves on heterogeneous targets; one silent failure quietly corrupts a placebo gap and hence the p-value. Cheap to guard, and the notebook's inference layer rests on it.
  - **Fix.** After minimize: assert/warn on not res.success; clip to [0,1] and renormalize w /= w.sum(); optionally retry from a random simplex start on failure.

- **⚪ POLISH · statistical** — `Cell 11 first caveat and cell 21 ('synthetic control is mildly conservative')`
  - **Issue.** The claim that synthetic control is 'mildly conservative' (systematically underestimates) is asserted twice as the explanation for the 305-vs-339 shortfall, but no evidence in the notebook distinguishes systematic bias from single-seed sampling noise — there is exactly one simulation run.
  - **Evidence.** Single seed (SEED=3) throughout; no repeat-simulation cell; caveat presented as a general property of the method.
  - **Why it matters.** If the shortfall is one seed's draw, the caveat teaches a false general lesson; if it is genuine Dirichlet-shrinkage bias (plausible given eff_donors = 14.7 pulls the synthetic toward the average control), that is a specific, fixable property of THIS Bayesian variant, not of 'synthetic control'. Either way the sentence as written is unsupported.
  - **Fix.** Either soften to 'in this run the estimate came in ~10% low' pending the multi-seed check, or run the 20-seed recovery loop and report the actual bias and coverage, attributing any systematic shortfall to the Dirichlet prior rather than to synthetic control per se.

---

### src/cmp — statistical core (estimators, metrics)

**Grade: adequate** · 1 critical · 7 major · 12 findings total

The statistical core is thoughtfully engineered — the uniform (n_samples, n_units) posterior CATE interface, the correctly handled and regression-tested pymc-bart sample_vars gotcha, a textbook-correct AIPW score, and a proper Abadie-style placebo permutation p-value are real strengths. But it is not yet watertight. One critical bug: the E-value fallback branch (the branch headline notebook 01 actually uses) is statistically meaningless and directionally inverted — an effect barely above break-even yields E~173 while an effect far above break-even yields E~1.7, and cost=0 degenerates to E~1 for any effect size; notebook 01's committed output narrates the bogus 5.76 with the standard VanderWeele risk-ratio interpretation. Beyond that: AIPW's docstring claims trimming but the code clips propensities and drops nothing (n_trimmed counts units that are all kept), and there is no cross-fitting behind the influence-function SE; the Bayesian synthetic-control effect intervals omit observation noise; the "McCrary" test is a unit-scale-dependent histogram ratio with no inference; BCF's treatment forest inherits a pymc-bart prior calibrated to the outcome (centered near y.mean(), not 0) while its docstring's "near-nominal coverage" claim is contradicted by the committed 0.764 coverage in notebook 01; and convergence checking is suppressed at package level with no diagnostic surfaced in the BART notebooks. Tests guard the one gotcha well but are otherwise mostly smoke/ordering checks — the broken e_value branch and a McCrary manipulation-positive case are exactly the untested paths.

**Strengths.**
- The pymc-bart counterfactual gotcha (frozen predictions unless sample_vars=['mu'] is passed) is correctly handled in _bart_predict (estimators.py:59-73), loudly documented, and guarded by a real regression test (test_package.py:49-61) asserting a known-nonzero CATE — exactly the right defense for the package's highest-risk silent failure.
- The AIPW influence-function score (estimators.py:167-169) is the textbook doubly-robust formula, the bootstrap refits both nuisance models inside each replicate (correctly propagating nuisance uncertainty), and propensities are clipped for stability.
- placebo_in_space (estimators.py:299-322) implements Abadie-style permutation inference properly: each placebo excludes the truly treated unit from its donor pool, poor pre-fit placebos are filtered by the RMSE-multiple rule, and the p-value uses the add-one permutation correction.
- The Bayesian synthetic control uses Dirichlet simplex weights (estimators.py:277), which genuinely enforces the Abadie no-extrapolation/no-negative-weights property, and the docstring explains why that matters.
- The E-value sd-branch (metrics.py:124-126) uses the correct VanderWeele conversion RR=exp(0.91*d) for a continuous outcome and the correct E = RR + sqrt(RR*(RR-1)) formula.
- PEHE, interval coverage (with by-decile breakdown), sharpness, reliability curve, and uplift-by-decile are all implemented to standard definitions, and docstrings are honest about scope (e.g., qini_curve explicitly says it is the oracle-truth realised uplift, not an estimate; s_learner documents its own failure mode).
- Uniform (n_samples, n_units) posterior interface across S/T/BCF keeps notebooks swappable and downstream metrics estimator-agnostic — good architecture for a cookbook.

- **🔴 CRITICAL · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/metrics.py:128 (fallback branch of e_value), used in notebooks/01_uplift_targeting.ipynb cell 28`
  - **Issue.** The no-sd fallback of e_value computes rr = |estimate| / |estimate - cost|, which is not a risk-ratio conversion under any convention and is directionally INVERTED: an estimate barely above break-even (needing only a sliver of confounding to explain away) gets a huge E-value, while an estimate far above break-even gets a small one. Verified numerically: e_value(6.07, cost=6.0) = 172.9 (should be ~1.1; the correct sd-branch gives 1.13), e_value(6.07, cost=1.0) = 1.68 (should be large; sd-branch gives 4.47), and cost=0 always returns ~1.0 regardless of effect size because |est|/|est-0| = 1.
  - **Evidence.** metrics.py:128: rr = max(abs(estimate) / max(abs(estimate - cost), 1e-6), 1.0 + 1e-6). Reproduced: e_value(6.07,6.0)=172.93, e_value(6.07,1.0)=1.68, e_value(6.07,0)=1.001. Notebook 01 cell 28: evalue = metrics.e_value(tau.mean(), COST) with committed output 'E-value ~ 5.76'; sd-branch on the same inputs would give a very different number.
  - **Why it matters.** Notebook 01 — the flagship uplift notebook — calls metrics.e_value(tau.mean(), COST) with no sd, so it runs this broken branch, and its committed output prints 'E-value ~ 5.76 — a hidden confounder would need about a 5.8x association with BOTH email and spend (risk-ratio scale)'. That headline sensitivity number is fabricated by an inverted formula and would not survive one pointed question from anyone who knows VanderWeele-Ding. It also feeds the committed decision JSON (cell 32, e_value: 5.756).
  - **Fix.** Delete the fallback or replace it with a defensible mapping: either require sd (raise if absent), or for a strictly positive outcome scale use rr = estimate/cost (the multiplicative shrinkage needed to reach the threshold). Update notebook 01 to pass sd=df['y'].std() like notebook 05 does, re-run, and fix the narrated number. Add a unit test pinning e_value's direction on the no-sd path.
  - **Verifier.** Both prongs hold.

(a) Factual accuracy — verified against the artifacts:
- src/cmp/metrics.py:128 contains verbatim `rr = max(abs(estimate) / max(abs(estimate - cost), 1e-6), 1.0 + 1e-6)`, and this branch runs whenever sd is None (line 124 guard).
- notebooks/01_uplift_targeting.ipynb cell 28 (extract line 946) calls `evalue = metrics.e_value(tau.mean(), COST)` with no sd, so it hits the fallback; committed output (extract line 961) prints "E-value ~ 5.76 — a hidden confounder would need about a 5.8x association with BOTH email and spend (risk-ratio scale)", and the decision JSON (extract line 1053) commits "e_value": 5.756405386243919. Notebook 05 (extract line 226) passes sd=df["spend"].s…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:151-153, 161-162, 173-174, 182 (aipw_ate)`
  - **Issue.** Docstring and return dict describe trimming ('Overlap is enforced by trimming units with propensity outside [trim, 1-trim]', key n_trimmed), but the code trims nothing: it CLIPS estimated propensities to [0.02, 0.98] (line 162) and keeps every unit in the score; n_trimmed (line 174) merely counts units whose e is extreme, all of which remain in the estimate. Clipping weights and trimming the sample are different estimators — trimming changes the estimand to a trimmed-population ATE, clipping keeps the full-population ATE but biases the IPW correction for the clipped units.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:161-162: e = ...predict_proba(Xi)[:,1]; e = np.clip(e, trim, 1-trim) — no row filtering anywhere; psi at 167-169 is computed on all n units; 173-174 computes n_trim from a separate full-data propensity fit but idx is never restricted.
  - **Why it matters.** Any notebook or reader reporting 'n_trimmed units were trimmed for overlap' states something false about what was computed; and under genuine positivity violations the clipped AIPW estimate quietly absorbs bias on exactly the units the docstring claims were removed. For a lecture whose axis-1 bar includes overlap/positivity diagnostics, mislabeling the overlap fix is the kind of gap a sharp audience member pokes.
  - **Fix.** Either actually drop units with e outside [trim, 1-trim] before computing psi (and document the estimand change to a trimmed-population ATE), or rename the behavior honestly: parameter clip, return key n_clipped, docstring 'propensities winsorized to [clip, 1-clip]'.
  - **Verifier.** Factually accurate: estimators.py:151-153 docstring says overlap is enforced "by trimming units with propensity outside [trim, 1-trim]" and that the dict returns "the number of trimmed units"; but the code at line 162 only clips estimated propensities (e = np.clip(e, trim, 1-trim)) and never drops a row — psi (167-169) is computed on all units, _score is called with np.arange(n) (176) and full-range bootstrap indices (181). n_trim (173-174) is a count from a separate full-data propensity fit that never restricts the sample, despite the inline comment at 172 claiming "keep only units with adequate overlap". Statistically sound: trimming and clipping are different estimators — trimming changes…
  - **Corrected statement.** aipw_ate clips (winsorizes) estimated propensities to [trim, 1-trim] and keeps all n units in the AIPW score; it does not trim any units. The returned n_trimmed is only a count of units with extreme full-data propensities, all of which remain in the estimate. The docstring, the line-172 comment, and the return key should say clipping/n_clipped — or the code should actually drop those units and document the resulting trimmed-population estimand.

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:159-178 (aipw_ate nuisance fitting and SE)`
  - **Issue.** The outcome models (gradient-boosted trees) and propensity model are fit on the full sample and evaluated on the same sample — no cross-fitting/sample-splitting — yet the function reports the influence-function SE psi.std/sqrt(n) (line 178) and the docstring sells the doubly-robust consistency guarantee. With adaptive learners like GBMs, own-observation overfitting shrinks the residuals (y - mu_t) toward zero, reintroducing first-order plug-in bias and invalidating the IF-based SE; the DR/efficiency theory for ML nuisances requires cross-fitting (Chernozhukov et al. DML) or Donsker-class nuisances, which boosted trees are not. The bootstrap does not repair this: each replicate refits on data containing ~37% duplicated rows, amplifying overfitting.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:165-166: mu1 = m1.fit(Xi[Ti==1], yi[Ti==1]).predict(Xi) — the same Xi rows used for fitting are scored; no KFold anywhere; line 178: inf_se = psi_full.std(ddof=1)/np.sqrt(n) presented as 'the influence-function SE'.
  - **Why it matters.** AIPW is positioned as the model-agnostic cross-check that validates the BART estimates (notebooks 00 and 01 both plot its CI as the honest benchmark). If the cross-check itself has systematically too-small residual corrections and an unjustified SE, its agreement with BART is weaker evidence than the narrative claims — and 'why no cross-fitting?' is a standard question from anyone who knows double ML.
  - **Fix.** Add 2- or 5-fold cross-fitting inside _score (fit nuisances on K-1 folds, score the held-out fold) — ~10 lines, negligible runtime at these n — and note in the docstring that cross-fitting is what licenses the IF-based SE with boosted-tree nuisances.
  - **Verifier.** FACTUAL ACCURACY — verified against the artifact, all citations check out:

1. `src/cmp/estimators.py:159-170` (`_score`): the logistic propensity is fit on `Xi, Ti` and scored on the same `Xi` (line 161), and both GBMs are fit on the treated/control subsets of `Xi` and then `.predict(Xi)` on the full same-sample rows (lines 165-166). Exactly as the reviewer quotes.
2. No cross-fitting exists anywhere: `grep -rn "KFold|cross_val|crossfit|fold" src/cmp/` returns nothing.
3. Line 178: `inf_se = float(psi_full.std(ddof=1) / np.sqrt(n))`, returned as `"se"` and described in the docstring (line 153) as "the influence-function SE". The docstring (lines 148-149) explicitly sells double robustness (…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:213-226 (mccrary_density), consumed by notebooks/09_threshold_perk_rdd.ipynb cell 6`
  - **Issue.** Three problems with what the name promises. (1) The +1 additive smoothing is applied to DENSITY values (counts/bandwidth), so the statistic is not invariant to the units of the running variable: rescale spend from euros to cents (densities /100) and log((d_above+1)/(d_below+1)) collapses toward 0 even under blatant manipulation — e.g. densities 0.24 vs 0.48 (a genuine 2x pile-up) give log-ratio 0.18, below the notebook's 0.3 'no manipulation' threshold. (2) It compares average density over the full one-bandwidth window each side, so any slope in the density near the cutoff (guaranteed with a skewed spend distribution) mechanically shifts the log-ratio without any manipulation — McCrary's actual test uses local-linear density fits on each side precisely to remove that slope confound. (3) There is no standard error or p-value; the thresholds used (0.5 in test_package.py:164, 0.3 in notebook 09 cell 6) are arbitrary constants with no sampling-distribution justification.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:223-225: d_below = len(below)/max(bandwidth,1e-9); log_ratio = np.log((d_above+1)/(d_below+1)). Notebook 09 cell 6 output: 'density below 23.7 vs above 24.0, log-ratio +0.01' with hard threshold abs(log_ratio) < 0.3.
  - **Why it matters.** Notebook 09 headlines this as 'the McCrary density test', the validity check on which the RDD's causal claim rests, and prints a binary verdict ('no sign of sorting across the cutoff') from an inference-free, scale-dependent statistic. On the simulated data it happens to be fine (23.7 vs 24.0), but the README invites running on real data, where a slope-confounded or scale-shrunken log-ratio could pass a genuinely manipulated cutoff.
  - **Fix.** Minimum honest fix: drop the +1 (use counts directly with a binomial SE on the split — under no manipulation, N_above|N_total ~ Binomial(N_total, ~0.5) for symmetric windows — giving an actual p-value), rename to density_jump_check, and say explicitly it is a crude proxy that ignores slope. Better: fit local-linear densities each side (or wrap rddensity-style logic) so slope is removed; at minimum shrink the window or compare to a slope-extrapolated prediction.
  - **Verifier.** Factual accuracy verified: estimators.py:223-225 does add +1 to density values (counts/bandwidth) inside the log-ratio; test_package.py:164 asserts abs(log_ratio) < 0.5; notebook 09 cell 6 uses a hard abs(log_ratio) < 0.3 rule and its committed output is exactly 'density below 23.7 vs above 24.0, log-ratio +0.01 — no sign of sorting across the cutoff', with cells 4-7 presenting this as 'the McCrary density test' and concluding 'the RDD comparison is fair' with no proxy caveat visible to readers. Statistical soundness verified on all three points: (1) the +1 on densities makes the statistic unit-dependent — rescaling euros to cents shrinks densities 100x so +1 dominates and the log-ratio coll…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:286-296 (synthetic_control effect_samples)`
  - **Issue.** The posterior over the counterfactual (cf = W_post @ donors, line 287) uses only the weight posterior and discards the fitted observation noise sd. effect_samples = target - cf is therefore a posterior on the gap between the observed series and the counterfactual MEAN path, not a posterior predictive on the counterfactual outcome. Since the observed post-period target contains a draw of that same noise, credible intervals on the per-period effect are systematically too narrow — they omit the sigma-level term entirely (the model at lines 277-280 estimates sd on the pre-period and then never uses it).
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:286-288: W_post = idata.posterior['w']...; cf = W_post @ donors; effect = target[None,:] - cf — sd from idata.posterior is never added; the docstring (line 272) nonetheless calls this 'a posterior over the counterfactual path and hence the effect'.
  - **Why it matters.** The cookbook converts these effect samples into P(lift > 0) and euro decisions; understated interval width inflates P(worthwhile) and contradicts the project's own coverage/sharpness discipline ('sharp + miscalibrated is overconfidence', metrics.py:107). With weekly noise sd~5 and few post weeks, the omitted term is not negligible relative to the weight-uncertainty band. CausalPy's own WeightedSumFitter, by contrast, reports posterior-predictive intervals.
  - **Fix.** Add noise to the counterfactual draws: cf = W_post @ donors + rng.normal(0, sd_post[:,None], cf.shape) using the posterior sd draws (or expose both mean-path and predictive versions and make the notebooks state which one their P(lift>0) uses).
  - **Verifier.** Factual check: estimators.py:286-288 is exactly as cited — cf = W_post @ donors uses only the Dirichlet weight posterior; effect = target - cf; the HalfNormal sd fitted at line 278 is never used after sampling (not in cf, effect, or the returned dict). The docstring (line 260; reviewer cited 272, same docstring) does call this "a posterior over the counterfactual path and hence the effect." Notebook 07 consumes effect_samples raw: per-week 90% bands (cell 8), total_post = effect[:,post].sum(1), and policy.go_no_go yielding P_value_gt_cost=0.62 (cell 10), with no disclosure that the band is a mean-path band; tests only check mean recovery. Statistical check: the criticism is sound. The estima…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:128-136 (bcf) and its docstring at :110-111, cross-checked against notebooks/01_uplift_targeting.ipynb cell 32 output`
  - **Issue.** The treatment-effect forest is declared as pmb.BART('tau', X=Xtd, Y=y, m=m_tau) — pymc-bart uses Y to set the sum-of-trees initialization and leaf-value prior scale, so tau's implied prior is anchored near y.mean() at the full outcome scale, not centered at 0 with tight shrinkage as Hahn-Murray-Carvalho prescribe for the moderating forest. 'Fewer trees' (line 122) limits tree depth complexity but does not re-center or re-scale the prior. With the FAST profile (150 draws/150 tune) the sampler has limited budget to pull tau away from its y-scale anchoring. Separately, the docstring's calibration claim — 'Near-nominal CATE interval coverage under observational selection' (lines 110-111) — is contradicted by the committed evidence: notebook 01's decision JSON reports coverage90 = 0.764 for this BCF.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:128: tau_b = pmb.BART('tau', X=Xtd, Y=y, m=m_tau) — same Y=y as the prognostic forest; notebooks/01_uplift_targeting.ipynb cell 32 committed output: 'coverage90': 0.7644 vs docstring 'near-nominal CATE interval coverage'; test_package.py:72: assert cate.mean() > 0.
  - **Why it matters.** BCF is sold in the notebooks as the coverage-calibrated estimator, and the whole point of the original BCF tau prior is aggressive shrinkage toward homogeneity/zero. A y.mean()-anchored tau prior can bias CATEs toward the outcome level under short chains, and the package's own committed output shows 90% intervals covering only 76% — a 14-point shortfall stated on the same page as the 'near-nominal' claim. The existing test (test_package.py:64-72) cannot catch any of this because it only asserts cate.mean() > 0, which a y.mean()-biased tau would pass spuriously (y.mean() > 0).
  - **Fix.** Center the moderating forest: fit tau's BART against a zero-centered pseudo-response or residual scale (e.g. Y=y - y.mean(), or pass a response proxy with mean 0 and SD ~ a prior effect scale) so its leaf prior shrinks toward no effect; soften the docstring coverage claim or qualify it as full-sampling-only; strengthen the test to compare cate.mean() against the DGP's known ATE within a tolerance and check coverage is within a sane band.
  - **Verifier.** I attempted to refute each element of the finding and failed on every substantive point.

(a) Factual accuracy — all citations check out:
1. src/cmp/estimators.py:127-128: `prog = pmb.BART("prog", X=Xpd, Y=y, m=p["m"])` and `tau_b = pmb.BART("tau", X=Xtd, Y=y, m=m_tau)` — the moderating forest is indeed fit with the same raw outcome `Y=y` as the prognostic forest, and line 122's only "shrinkage" device is `m_tau = max(10, p["m"] // 2)`.
2. The mechanism claim is confirmed in the installed pymc-bart source (.venv/lib/python3.12/site-packages/pymc_bart/pgbart.py): line 205 `init_mean = self.Y.mean()`; line 224 initializes the entire sum-of-trees at `init_mean`; line 228 sets initial leaf value…

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:54, :67-68, :134, :284 (compute_convergence_checks=False everywhere; warnings suppressed in _bart_predict)`
  - **Issue.** All three PyMC fitters (_fit_bart, bcf, synthetic_control) pass compute_convergence_checks=False, and _bart_predict wraps scoring in warnings.simplefilter('ignore'). Combined with the FAST default (draws=150, tune=150, chains=2 -> 300 posterior draws), no r-hat/divergence/ESS warning can ever surface from the package, and a grep shows the BART-based notebooks (00, 01, 02, 03, 07) contain no rhat/ess/az.summary check of their own — only the legacy CausalPy notebooks (04-06, 08-11) do.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:54, :134, :284: compute_convergence_checks=False; :67-68: warnings.catch_warnings(); warnings.simplefilter('ignore'); grep over notebooks for 'rhat|ess_bulk|az.summary' matches only 04, 05, 06, 08, 09, 10, 11 — none of the cmp-core BART notebooks.
  - **Why it matters.** The review bar explicitly treats visible-but-unaddressed convergence problems as top-priority; here the problem is one level worse — convergence problems are made structurally invisible for exactly the notebooks whose committed outputs (e.g. BCF coverage 0.764, 90% quantiles from 300 draws) most plausibly suffer from under-sampling. A lecture-grade figure could be silently built on unconverged chains and nobody, including the author, would see a warning.
  - **Fix.** Keep the flag off for speed if needed, but add a small cmp diagnostic helper (max r-hat, min bulk-ESS from az.summary on the key vars) and call it in every BART notebook right after sampling, with a printed pass/fail line; in FAST mode print an explicit 'under-sampled, indicative only' banner so committed outputs are honestly labeled.

- **🟠 MAJOR · code** — `tests/test_package.py (whole file) and tests/test_notebooks.py:35`
  - **Issue.** Test coverage is thin exactly where the statistics are fragile. Meaningfully tested: the BART gotcha (49-61), PEHE exactness (100-103), interval coverage on a calibrated synthetic (89-97), AIPW recovery within +/-1.5 (147-151), SC recovery within a factor of ~2 (75-83). Merely smoke/ordering tests: auuc (only good > bad and <= 1.01; a perfect ranker should score ~1.0 and is never checked), first_stage_F (only strong > 10 > weak ordering; never checked against a hand-computed or statsmodels F on fixed data), e_value (only monotonicity on the sd branch — the broken no-sd fallback, the one notebook 01 uses, is completely untested), mccrary (only the null/no-manipulation case; no positive case asserting a real pile-up is flagged, no scale-invariance test), bcf (only cate.mean() > 0). Entirely untested: reliability_curve, sharpness, ate_bias, corr_with_truth, qini_curve internals, placebo_in_space (p-value correctness, the RMSE filter, and the empty-placebo crash), sc_weights_slsqp (an exact test exists: target equal to one donor must return weight ~1 on it), propensity_scores, and every CausalPy wrapper (test_notebooks.py only asserts notebooks execute without exception — zero output assertions).
  - **Evidence.** test_package.py:167-168 tests only e_value(10,0,sd=5) > e_value(2,0,sd=5) — sd branch only; :161-164 only the no-manipulation McCrary case; :72 assert cate.mean() > 0 for BCF; tests/test_notebooks.py:35 client.execute() with no output checks.
  - **Why it matters.** For a package whose stated bar is 'watertight statistics', the tests as written would pass unchanged if e_value's fallback were inverted (it is), if mccrary never detected manipulation, if BCF were biased to y.mean(), or if a CausalPy version bump silently changed a wrapper's estimand — i.e., they do not defend the claims the lecture makes.
  - **Fix.** Add known-value tests: auuc(tau, tau) == 1.0 within tolerance and auuc of a reversed ranker < 0; first_stage_F against statsmodels OLS F on a fixed small dataset; e_value against VanderWeele's published example (RR=3.9 -> E=7.26) plus a direction test on the fallback; a McCrary positive case (inject a 2x pile-up above cutoff, assert log-ratio flags it) and a scale-invariance case; BCF recovery of the known ATE within tolerance; sc_weights_slsqp exact-donor recovery; placebo_in_space p-value near uniform under a null DGP and the all-filtered edge case.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `src/cmp/metrics.py:45-72 (qini_curve, auuc) and notebooks/01 committed output 'AUUC': 0.8996`
  - **Issue.** Naming conflates two conventions. What auuc returns when normalized (the default) is area-above-the-random-line divided by the oracle's area-above-random — i.e., a Qini-coefficient-style ratio (Radcliffe's Q) — while 'AUUC' conventionally denotes the raw area under the uplift curve. Additionally, both functions require tau_true, so the package contains no observed-outcome Qini (cumulative treated-vs-control response difference) at all — the only version computable on the real Hillstrom data the README advertises.
  - **Evidence.** metrics.py:68-71: area_model = trapz(cum_model - cum_random); return area_model/area_oracle — this is the oracle-normalized Qini coefficient, printed in notebook 01's decision JSON under the key 'AUUC'.
  - **Why it matters.** A student who later computes AUUC with scikit-uplift/causalml on real data will get numbers on a different scale than the 0.90 shown in the lecture and conclude something regressed; and the 'run it on real data' promise cannot be kept for the ranking diagnostic because the oracle-truth version is the only one implemented.
  - **Fix.** Rename the normalized quantity qini_coefficient (or document 'normalized AUUC (Qini-coefficient convention)' everywhere it is printed), and add an observed-outcome qini_observed(cate_point, T, y) for the real-data mode.

- **🟡 MINOR · code** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:315-321 (placebo_in_space)`
  - **Issue.** Two edge/consistency landmines: (1) if every placebo is discarded by the RMSE filter (small donor pool + tight rmse_multiple), placebo_gaps becomes an empty 1-D array and placebo_gaps[:, post] raises IndexError instead of a clear message; (2) the filter compares SLSQP placebo pre-RMSEs against a pre_rmse argument that notebook callers take from the Bayesian fit (posterior-mean gap RMSE) — two different fitters, so the Abadie 'x times the treated unit's fit' rule is applied across non-comparable objectives and can over- or under-filter.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:315: placebo_gaps = np.array(placebo_gaps) then :320 placebo_gaps[:, post] — shape (0,) cannot be indexed with two axes; the function computes real_gap itself via SLSQP (:318) yet takes pre_rmse as an external argument rather than deriving it from that same SLSQP fit.
  - **Why it matters.** (1) is a live crash path on real data with few donors; (2) quietly changes which placebos enter the permutation p-value, which is the headline inference of the synthetic-control notebook.
  - **Fix.** Compute pre_rmse internally from the SLSQP real_gap (drop the argument or keep it only as an override), and raise a ValueError with guidance ('all placebos filtered; increase rmse_multiple') when the kept list is empty.

- **🟡 MINOR · statistical** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:196-210 (first_stage_F)`
  - **Issue.** The F-statistic is the classical homoskedastic one (computed from R^2), and the docstring anchors it to the Stock-Yogo F<10 rule. That rule is only valid under homoskedasticity; with heteroskedastic real data (the package ships real-data loaders) the modern standard is the heteroskedasticity-robust effective F of Montiel Olea-Pflueger, and a homoskedastic F>10 can coexist with a weak instrument.
  - **Evidence.** estimators.py:207-209: r2 = 1 - rss/tss; F = (r2/k)/((1-r2)/(n-k-1)) — no robust variance option; docstring cites 'Stock-Yogo rule of thumb: F < 10'.
  - **Why it matters.** Fine for the simulated IV notebook, but the cookbook explicitly doubles as a toolkit for real data, where quoting 'F=XX > 10, instrument is strong' from this function is an over-claim a knowledgeable audience member can challenge.
  - **Fix.** Add one docstring sentence ('homoskedastic F; on real data prefer a robust/effective F — F>10 here is necessary, not sufficient') or add a robust=True option using HC-robust SEs on the instrument coefficient (F = t^2).

- **⚪ POLISH · consistency** — `src/cmp/estimators.py:13`
  - **Issue.** The module docstring says the gotcha guard lives in tests/test_estimators.py, but no such file exists — the test is test_bart_cate_is_nonzero in tests/test_package.py:49.
  - **Evidence.** ls tests/ shows only test_notebooks.py and test_package.py; estimators.py:13 references 'tests/test_estimators.py'.
  - **Why it matters.** Anyone auditing the guard (the single most important test per the package's own docs) is pointed at a nonexistent file.
  - **Fix.** Change the docstring reference to tests/test_package.py::test_bart_cate_is_nonzero.

---

### src/cmp — simulators, policy math, data loaders, plots

**Grade: adequate** · 2 critical · 6 major · 14 findings total

The shared package is well-architected: every simulator threads a local seeded Generator (no global state), the planted truths almost always match the estimand later validated, the VOI/EVPI math is textbook-correct, oracles are fairly defined, and the data loaders were verified live with correct schemas. But the watertight-statistics bar is broken in two places with committed evidence: (1) a seed collision between dgp.uplift_customers and policy.policy_comparison makes the "random-50%" baseline in the Anchor A policy table a disguised recency-targeting rule (+€1,414 shown where a true random half must average about −€870 — a ~10-sigma impossibility that an alert student can spot because "random half" outperforms zero while treat-all loses money), and (2) the Depth A bake-off read-out asserts BCF wins on recovery, ranking and coverage while its own committed table shows the T-learner ahead on all three. Around these sit several majors: go_no_go labels absolute net value "expected_roi"; the MMM simulator's planted economics put TRUE TV ROI at ~0.85x, contradicting the notebook's "TV clears break-even, trust the ROI verdict" headline; dag_control_demo's docstring/DAG story about opened_email contradicts both its own code and pathmc's printed "OK — no collider opened"; and mojibake (â‚¬, Ï„) is baked into the axis labels of most lecture figures. All are precisely locatable and cheap to fix; none require re-architecting.

**Strengths.**
- Seeding discipline is exemplary in itself: every dgp function constructs a local np.random.default_rng(seed), covariates are drawn before regime branching (so randomized/observational twins share the same true tau, as 00_foundations claims), and multi-seed stability loops (nb01 cell 17) work cleanly with no hidden global state.
- Ground-truth/estimand alignment is mostly exact: segment_customers returns segment truths evaluated at mean engagement (7.0/16.0) which is precisely what nb02 validates against; funnel's product-of-coefficients path truths (3.17/0.77/3.94) match the fitted linear-SCM path queries; did_rollout/rdd_perk/its_redesign plant exactly the step/jump/lift their notebooks estimate; price_panel's simultaneity is engineered with the correct bias direction (toward zero) and nb03 demonstrates it (-0.59 vs true -1.42).
- policy.value_of_information is a correct per-customer EVPI decomposition, E[max(tau-c,0)] - max(E[tau]-c,0) >= 0, with the straddler diagnosis coherently tied to the 90% interval; oracle policies are fairly defined as true tau > cost; cost/confidence sweeps are honestly evaluated on the planted truth, not on the model's own predictions.
- data.py verified live: the robjellis LaLonde CSV headers (treat, age, educ, black, hispan, married, nodegree, re74, re75, re78) exactly match nb05's adjustment set; the CEVAE IHDP file has exactly the 30 columns the loader names; the Hillstrom URL returns 200; licence notes are present; there are no silent row drops, and hillstrom_uplift correctly restricts to pre-treatment covariates with the right no-ground-truth caveat spelled out.
- plots.py defaults are honest: coverage bars pinned to [0,1] with the nominal line, 45-degree references on recovery/reliability, random and oracle reference curves on Qini plots, credible bands on the SC counterfactual, dashed-black-truth convention everywhere — no truncated axes or unlabeled uncertainty.

- **🔴 CRITICAL · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/policy.py:92,115 with notebooks/01_uplift_targeting.ipynb cells 3 and 19 (extract 01 lines 122, 576, 591-599)`
  - **Issue.** The 'random-50%' baseline in policy_comparison is not random in the committed Anchor A results: nb01 seeds both dgp.uplift_customers(seed=7) and policy.policy_comparison(..., seed=SEED=7). Both build np.random.default_rng(7), and Generator.uniform(5,330,n) (dgp.py:42, recency) consumes the identical underlying doubles as Generator.random(n) (policy.py:115), so rand_mask is exactly (recency < 167.5) — a recency-targeting rule that preferentially selects the persuadables whose tau bump is centred at recency 120 (dgp.py:49).
  - **Evidence.** Committed table (nb01 cell 19): treat-all profit = -1734 over 900 customers (mean gain -1.93), so a genuinely random half must average about -867; the table shows random-50% = +1414, a ~2,280 swing that is roughly 10 sigma under true randomness — and matches the expected profit of the 'recency<167.5' rule almost exactly. The rendered policy_bar figure shows the internally impossible pattern 'random half earns +1,414 while treating everyone loses 1,734'.
  - **Why it matters.** This is the flagship euro-decision table of the lecture. It materially misstates the value of the random baseline (random targeting looks profitable when it destroys value), corrupts every 'model vs random' comparison downstream, and an alert MBA student can falsify it from the treat-all row alone.
  - **Fix.** Decouple the streams: in policy_comparison derive the mask from an independent stream (e.g. np.random.default_rng([seed, 0x9E3779B9]) or SeedSequence(seed).spawn(1)[0]), or accept a Generator; in nb01 stop passing the DGP seed. Re-execute and re-commit the table/figure; sanity-assert random-50% profit is about half of treat-all.
  - **Verifier.** Both prongs hold. (a) Factually accurate: dgp.py:41-42 draws recency as the FIRST n variates of default_rng(seed); policy.py:106,115 draws rand_mask as the FIRST n variates of default_rng(seed); nb01 passes seed=SEED=7 to both (extract lines 82, 122, 576), and the committed cell-19 table shows treat-all=-1734, random-50%=+1414 as quoted. (b) Technically sound, verified empirically with the repo's .venv (read-only numpy run): NumPy Generator.uniform(5,330,n) and Generator.random(n) consume identical underlying doubles (uniform == 5+325*random to 5.7e-14), so rand_mask is bit-for-bit equal to (recency < 167.5) — np.array_equal returned True, selecting 455/900. Since the persuadable tau bump (d…
  - **Corrected statement.** Minor refinement only: the coupling is ~14 sigma (not ~10), and it corrupts only the random-50% row and comparisons made against it — the treat-all, model, and oracle rows are computed independently of the coupled RNG stream and remain correct.

  - **Issue.** The Depth A bake-off read-out claims, for the observational world, that 'BCF gets the closest per-customer recovery, the best ranking, and near-nominal coverage' — but the committed table directly above shows the T-learner ahead on every one of those metrics: PEHE 5.85 vs BCF 6.34, corr 0.84 vs 0.83, AUUC tied at 0.90, cov90 0.93 (near-nominal) vs BCF 0.76 (severe undercoverage). Step 5 prints the same BCF numbers ('90% coverage 76%', line 400; decision JSON coverage90=0.764, line 1048). No markdown anywhere addresses the 76%: the caveats cell (lines 647-660) never mentions coverage, and the sole FAST-mode note (line 1023) says the posterior is 'coarse, so the straddler set is wide' — wide intervals would raise coverage, so it doe…
  - **Corrected statement.** In the committed FAST-mode outputs, the observational-world bake-off shows the T-learner, not BCF, with the best per-customer recovery (PEHE 5.85 vs 6.34; corr 0.84 vs 0.83), tied ranking (AUUC 0.90 both), and near-nominal coverage (0.93 vs BCF's 0.76 at a 90% level); BCF's genuine advantages here are lower ATE bias (0.49 vs 1.00) and agreement with AIPW, and its 76% coverage is unflagged undercoverage (plausibly FAST-mode under-sampling and/or shrinkage) that the notebook should explicitly caveat.

- **🟠 MAJOR · business** — `src/cmp/policy.py:166-180 (go_no_go), surfaced in notebooks/07 cell 10 (extract 07 lines 287-296)`
  - **Issue.** go_no_go computes roi = total_effect_samples - cost and reports its mean as 'expected_roi'. That is absolute expected net value, not ROI. The committed nb07 decision JSON prints expected_value 304.99, cost 300.0, 'expected_roi': 4.99 — the actual expected ROI is 305/300 - 1 = 1.7% (or 1.02x), not 4.99 anything.
  - **Evidence.** policy.py:171 'roi = total_effect_samples - cost'; nb07 cell 10 output: {"expected_value": 304.99, "expected_roi": 4.99, "cost": 300.0}.
  - **Why it matters.** For an MBA audience ROI is a ratio by definition; a JSON slide showing 'expected_roi: 4.99' next to value 305 and cost 300 reads as 499% ROI and invites an immediate, credibility-denting correction from the room.
  - **Fix.** Rename the key to expected_net_value (and keep it), and add a true ratio: expected_roi = float((total_effect_samples / cost).mean() - 1) or median ROI, plus P(ROI>0). Regenerate nb07's committed output.

- **🟠 MAJOR · statistical — ✅ verified** — `src/cmp/dgp.py:228-247 (dag_control_demo) with notebooks/05 cells 5, 8-11 (extract 05 lines 93-96, 139-146, 160-210)`

  - **Verifier.** All cited facts verified against the artifacts. - **🟠 MAJOR · business** — `src/cmp/dgp.py:279-294 (mmm_weekly) with notebooks/06 cells 7-13 (extract 06 lines 165, 216-234, 259-271)`
  - **Issue.** The MMM simulator's planted economics put the TRUE TV ROI below break-even: true TV contribution 3,536 against total TV spend of about 4,160 (mean 40/week x 104 weeks) gives ~0.85x, and brand_search ~0.13x. Yet the notebook's headline is 'TV's interval sits above the break-even line (1x) ... which is the whole budget story' and the repeated thesis is 'trust the ranking and the ROI verdict, not the absolute euros'. The 'TV clears break-even' verdict only holds because the fitted contribution (11,775) is 3.3x the truth — the very miscalibration the notebook tells you to distrust. The caveat 'they ran ~2-3x the truth' also understates the committed outputs (TV 3.3x, brand_search 6.3x).
  - **Evidence.** nb06 cell 3: 'TRUE incremental sales — TV 3,536'; cell 7: 'Causal MMM contribution — TV 11,775'; cell 9: 'ROI — TV 2.81x'; dgp.py:293 saturate(adstock(tv_spend,0.4), alpha=60, kappa=50) with tv_spend ~ 40/week caps weekly TV contribution at ~34 per ~40 spent.

Only the ranking claim survives. A student who divides the printed true contributions by spend catches this in one line.
  - **Fix.** Retune the DGP so the intended verdict is true in the ground truth (e.g. alpha=120 for TV, or halve TV spend scale, so true TV ROI ~ 1.7x while brand_search stays ~0.1x), or introduce an explicit margin/unit-value so the break-even reference is not implicitly 1.0; and correct '~2-3x' to the actual 3.3x/6.3x. Re-commit outputs.

- **🟠 MAJOR · consistency** — `src/cmp/policy.py:91-136 (policy_comparison docstring vs code) with notebooks/01 cell 18 (extract 01 lines 555-562)`
  - **Issue.** The docstring promises things the function does not do: 'each with a posterior over profit induced by CATE uncertainty' (all masks are fixed (1,n) matrices; profit is a single deterministic number), 'Returns a DataFrame with mean profit, 90% CI, and fraction contacted' (no CI columns exist), and lists a 'treat-none: baseline (0)' policy that is absent from the policies dict. nb01 cell 18 inherits the error, promising 'five rules' then bulleting six including treat-none, while the committed table has five rows and no treat-none.
